The Four Sub-Arguments on Human Agency
Sanusi enumerates the four sub-arguments summarising all preceding proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main arguments used by those who claim humans create their own actions, as summarised from fifteen earlier proofs.

Core Idea
Fifteen arguments reduce to four key claims about power, will, and moral responsibility.

After presenting and refuting fifteen separate arguments, the author now summarises them into four core sub-arguments. These four capture the essence of the rationalist case for human agency. In this lesson, we will identify each of the four and see how they interrelate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice how the author introduces the summary.
The passage begins with 'فهذا مجموع كلام القوم في هذا الباب' — signalling that what follows is a condensation.
2
Identify the four sub-arguments
Locate the phrases 'أولها', 'ثانيها', 'ثالثها', 'رابعها'. For each, write down the core claim in your own words.
These correspond to intuitive, metaphysical, moral, and theological proof types.
3
Understand the author's intent
Recognise that these are the opponents' arguments, not the author's own position. The author considers them weak (شبهات) and will refute them.
The phrase 'شبهات أربع' labels them as specious arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا مجموع كلام القوم في هذا الباب واعلم: أن هذه الوجوه - وإن كثرت - إلا أن حاصلها راجع إلى شبهات أربعة: أولها: إن وجود أفعالنا دائر مع حصول قصورنا وإرادتنا، فوجب أن يكون وقوع هذه الأفعال بها وثانيها: إن المكنة من الفعل والترك، أمر وجداني اضطراري، وكون الله تعالى موجداً لأفعال العباد، يبطل هذا الوجدان وثالثها: إن العبد لو لم يكن موجداً لأفعال نفسه، يلزم أن يكون تكليفه جارياً مجرى تكليف العاجز. وذلك قبيح في العقل. ولزم كونه تعالى خالقاً لهذه القبائح، وبشتم نفسه. وكل ذلك قبيح في العقول. ورابعها: إنه لو لم يكن العبد موجدا لأفعال نفسه، لزم العجز عن إثبات الإله، وعن إثبات النبوة، وعن كون القرآن معجزاً. وكل ذلك باطل.
So this is the totality of the discourse of the people in this chapter. And know that these arguments — though numerous — their essence returns to four sub-arguments: The first: that the existence of our actions is concomitant with the occurrence of our power and will, so it must be that these actions occur by them. The second: that the ability to do or not do is an involuntary intuitive matter, and that God the Exalted is the originator of the actions of servants nullifies this intuition. The third: that if the servant is not the originator of his own actions, then his obligation runs along the same lines as obligating the incapable. That is intellectually repugnant. And it would necessitate that the Exalted be the creator of these evil acts, and revile Himself. All of that is repugnant to intellects. The fourth: that if the servant is not the originator of his own actions, then there would be inability to prove God, to prove prophethood, and to prove that the Qur'an is a miracle. All of that is false.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage serves as a summary and classification of the previous fifteen arguments. The author states that despite the multitude of proofs, they all reduce to four main categories. The first points to the direct correlation between our power/will and our actions: since our actions only occur when we have power and will, they must be caused by us, not by God directly. The second appeals to our intuitive self-awareness: we feel we have the ability to do or not do an action; if God were the sole creator, that feeling would be false. The third argues on moral grounds: if we do not create our own actions, then God's commands would be like commanding an incapable person, which is unjust (qabīḥ). Moreover, God would then be the creator of evil deeds and would even be 'insulting Himself' (i.e., commanding what He himself creates). The fourth argues that without human agency, we cannot prove God's existence, prophethood, or the Qur'an's miraculous nature, because these proofs rely on human actions (e.g., the miracles of prophets are performed by humans). Each of these sub-arguments is considered a shubha (specious argument) by the author, and they are all deemed false, but the summary presents them as the core of the opponent's case.

Key Terms
شبهات
وجدان
تكليف
إثبات الإله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments are the author's own conclusion.
Correction: The author labels them as 'شبهات' (specious arguments) and is summarising the opponents' case for refutation.

Checkpoint
According to the summary, what is the first sub-argument?
A) The intuitive feeling of ability to do or not do.
B) The correlation between our actions and our power and will.
C) The impossibility of proving God without human agency.
D) The injustice of obligating the incapable.

Key Takeaway
The four sub-arguments summarise the rationalist case for human free will, appealing to experience, intuition, morality, and theological proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do good deeds, do you subtly credit yourself and your own power, forgetting that God is the ultimate creator of all actions? How does this lesson's classification of such reasoning as a 'shubhah' challenge your inner pride? Will you consciously attribute every action—good or bad—to God's creation from now on?
?
The third sub-argument claims that if God created our evil acts, He would be reviling Himself. Have you ever secretly felt that God is unfair for decreeing suffering or sin? How does knowing that Sanusi refutes this argument help you submit to God's wisdom even when you don't understand His decree? Take a moment to repent of any subtle accusation against Allah's justice.
?
The fourth sub-argument warns that without human agency, one cannot prove prophethood or the Quran's miracle. Do you rely on your own intellectual arguments to validate your faith, rather than on the Quran itself as a divine sign? Are you willing to let go of the need to 'prove' everything intellectually and instead accept the Quran's miraculous nature as sufficient evidence?

Refuting the First Argument: Divine Causation of Acts
The author refutes the first Mu'tazili argument by presenting a dilemma and an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the first Mu'tazili argument by showing it leads to determinism or absurdity.

Core Idea
If motives are from God, then God is the agent of evil, not humans.

The Mu'tazila argue that human actions are caused by motives which are from God. The author responds by showing that if motives necessitate actions, then determinism follows and God becomes the agent of evil. If not, the argument collapses. He also challenges the necessity of the correlation using analogies from natural processes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili dilemma
Read the passage and identify the two possibilities the author presents: either the presence of motives necessitates the action, or it does not.
This sets up the refutation.
2
Follow the first horn: necessity leads to jabr
If the Mu'tazila claim necessity, then motives are from God, making the action unavoidable and obligating the impossible. Also, God becomes the agent of evil acts.
The author argues there is no difference between doing evil and causing what necessitates evil.
3
Consider the second horn: no necessity invalidates the argument
If they deny necessity, then the correlation is not proven, and their entire inference collapses.
This is a logical refutation.
4
Examine the second response: analogy of natural processes
The author denies the supposed necessity by comparing actions to satiety, quenching, and plant growth—things that are not always necessary despite conditions.
The text cuts off here, but the point is to challenge the self-evidence of the Mu'tazili claim.

Source Text

الأول : إنكم إما أن تدعوا وجوب حصول الفعل عند حصول الداعي ، وامتناع حصول الفعل عند عدم تلك الدواعي . وإما أن لا تدعوا ذلك . فإن ادعيتم الأول لزمكم الجبر ، لأن حصول تلك الدواعي ليست من العبد . وإلا لزم التسلسل ، بل تكون من الله تعالى

الثاني : أن نقول : لا نسلم أن حصول هذه الأفعال عند توفر الدواعي واجب . وذلك لأنه ليس حصول الأفعال عند حصول الدواعي أظهر من حصول الشبع للحي السليم ، عند تناول الغذاء الجيد . وحصول الري عقيب الشرب، وحصول النبت عند إلقاء البذر في الأرض الحرة ، مع حصول سائر الشرائط من السقي والشمس. ثم إن الأكثرين من المعتزلة : سلموا أن
And the answer to what they argued first in some aspects: First: Either you claim the necessity of the occurrence of the act upon the occurrence of the motive, and the impossibility of the occurrence of the act in the absence of those motives. Or you do not claim that. If you claim the first, determinism (jabr) would be necessary for you, because the occurrence of those motives is not from the slave. Otherwise, infinite regress would follow; rather, they are from Allah, exalted. On this basis: if Allah creates in the slave those motives, the occurrence of that act becomes necessary, whether the slave wills or refuses [and if He does not create them in him, the occurrence of the act becomes impossible, whether the slave wills or refuses]. And if this is so, then obligating the slave would be an obligation of what is not within his capacity. Also, because He, exalted, since He is the agent of what necessitates the occurrence of these ugly acts, He would also be the agent of these ugly acts. For there is no difference between doing the ugly act, and that which necessitates the ugly act, or doing that upon which the ugly act is consequent. As for if you do not say with the necessity of the occurrence of the act upon the occurrence of the will, and its impossibility upon the absence of the will, then this inference is not complete for you. Because if the occurrence of the act is not necessary upon the occurrence of motives, it does not follow that the non-occurrence of the act when motives are present, nor its occurrence when deterrents are absent, is impossible. And then this proof is entirely invalidated. Second: We say: We do not concede that the occurrence of these actions upon the presence of motives is necessary. For the occurrence of actions upon the occurrence of motives is not more evident than the occurrence of satiety in a healthy being upon eating good food, and the occurrence of quenching upon drinking, and the occurrence of plants upon casting seeds in good soil, with the presence of other conditions like watering and sun. Then most of the Mu'tazila conceded that
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (جواب) of the first Mu'tazili argument, which claimed that actions correlate with motives and will. The author presents two points. First: He poses a dilemma to the opponent: either they claim that the presence of motives necessitates action and their absence makes it impossible, or they do not. If they claim necessity, then they are committed to determinism (jabr) because motives are not created by the slave but by God. If God creates the motives, the action necessarily follows regardless of the slave's will, making obligation impossible (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). Moreover, God becomes the agent of evil acts by creating the motives that necessitate them, as there is no difference between doing evil and causing what necessitates evil. If they deny necessity, then the correlation is not proven, and their argument collapses. Second: The author denies that the correlation between motives and actions is necessary, using analogies: satiety from eating, quenching from drinking, and plants from seeds are not always necessary, so the Mu'tazili claim is not self-evident. The text cuts off here.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that motives from God necessitate human actions without making God the agent of evil.
Correction: The author shows that if motives necessitate actions and come from God, then God is the agent of evil acts, and determinism follows.

Checkpoint
According to the first response, if the Mu'tazila claim that motives necessitate actions, what follows?
A) The slave has free will.
B) Determinism (jabr) and God becomes the agent of evil.
C) The argument remains valid.
D) The slave can choose independently.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the Mu'tazili argument by showing it either leads to determinism or is logically invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever tell yourself, 'I couldn't help it because my desire was too strong'? The author argues that even if motives are from God, you are still accountable. Will you stop excusing your sins and take full responsibility before Allah?
?
Imagine that Allah created in you a motive that made it almost certain that you would commit a sin. Would you still see His judgment as just? How does this passage challenge your trust in Allah's justice, and how will you reconcile His decree with your accountability?
?
The author says there is no difference between doing evil and causing what necessitates evil. Think about your influence on others—do you create situations that lead them to sin? Name one specific way you will change your behavior this week to stop enabling evil in those around you.

The Dilemma of Necessity: First Argument Against Motive-Based Compulsion
The author presents a dilemma against the necessity of actions from motives, with two horns leading to compulsion or invalidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author forces a dilemma on opponents who claim motives necessitate actions, leading to the conclusion of compulsion and unjust divine obligation.

Core Idea
If motives make actions necessary, then either the servant is compelled or the argument collapses.

This lesson examines the first of three arguments against the objection that actions are necessitated by motives. The author presents a powerful dilemma: either the opponents claim necessity or they do not. If they do, they must accept compulsion (jabr) and the injustice of divine obligation beyond one's capacity. If they do not, their proof fails entirely. This argument cuts to the heart of the debate between free will and determinism.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma carefully
Focus on the two options presented: either actions are necessary when motives are present, or they are not. Notice how the author structures this as an 'if-then' argument.
The word 'إما' indicates an exclusive choice.
2
Explore the first horn: compulsion
Understand why claiming necessity leads to jabr (compulsion). The author argues that motives come from God, not the servant, so if they necessitate action, the servant has no choice.
Think about the implication for divine justice: how can God oblige what is beyond one's capacity?
3
Examine the second horn: invalidity
If the opponents do not claim necessity, their argument loses its force. Without necessary connection, they cannot prove that actions are determined by motives.
The author uses the term 'فساد' (invalidity) to show the argument collapses.
4
Reflect on the additional point about evil acts
Notice the author adds that if God creates the motives for evil actions, He becomes the agent of evil itself, since there is no difference between doing evil and causing what necessitates it.
This strengthens the reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the answer to what they argued first is in aspects: First: Either you claim the necessity of the action occurring when the motive occurs, and the impossibility of the action occurring in the absence of those motives. Or you do not claim that. If you claim the first, compulsion (jabr) is necessary for you, because the occurrence of those motives is not from the servant. Otherwise, infinite regress would be necessary; rather, they are from God, Most High. On this assumption: if God creates those motives in the servant, the occurrence of that action becomes necessary, whether the servant wills or refuses [and if He does not create them in him, the occurrence of the action is impossible, whether the servant wills or refuses]. And if it is like that, then charging the servant is charging him with what is beyond his capacity. Also: because He, Most High, since He is the agent of what necessitates the occurrence of these evil actions, He is also the agent of these evil deeds. For there is no difference between doing the evil and what necessitates the evil, or doing that upon which the evil necessarily follows. As for you not saying that the action is necessary upon the occurrence of the will, and impossible upon the absence of the will—then this argument is not established for you. Because if the action is not necessary upon the occurrence of the motives, it does not follow from the non-occurrence of the action when motives are complete and its occurrence when hindrances are absent: an impossibility. And then this proof is invalidated entirely. Second: We say: we do not concede that the occurrence of these actions when motives are complete is necessary. That is because the occurrence of actions upon the occurrence of motives is not more evident than the occurrence of satiety for a healthy living being upon consuming good food, the occurrence of quenching after drinking, and the occurrence of vegetation upon casting seed into fertile soil with the remaining conditions of watering and sunlight. Then most of the Mu'tazila conceded that the occurrence of these things after these matters is by way of habit, not by way of necessity. So too here. Third: Why is it not permissible to say: That action was necessarily occurring at that specific time in itself, and was impossible of occurrence in other times in itself and by its essence? This is not far-fetched according to the principles of the Mu'tazila. That is because according to them, accidents that do not subsist, the occurrence of each one is restricted to a specific time, even though its occurrence before or after that is impossible. So if the restriction of that permissibility to a time and not another is understood, why is it not also permissible that the necessity of the occurrence of these actions is restricted to a time and not another, without any effect at all? On this assumption, it is impossible to say: That agent indeed occurred by the servant's power. And on this specious argument are many principles which we have explained in the book 'Nihayat al-Uqul'.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation of the first objection raised by opponents (likely the Mu'tazila) who argue that actions are necessitated by motives (دواعٍ). The author presents three arguments (وجوه) in response. 

First argument: The author forces a dilemma on the opponents. Either they claim that when motives are present the action necessarily occurs, and in their absence it is impossible, or they do not claim this. If they claim the first, they must accept compulsion (جبر) because the motives are not within the servant's power—they come from God. If God creates the motives, the action occurs necessarily whether the servant wills or not; if not, it is impossible. This makes divine obligation (تكليف) impossible, as it demands what is beyond one's capacity. Moreover, if God is the agent of what necessitates evil acts, He is effectively the agent of evil itself, since causing what necessitates evil is akin to doing evil. If the opponents do not claim necessity, their argument collapses because without necessity they cannot prove their point. 

Second argument: The author denies the premise that actions are necessary given motives. He draws an analogy: the relation between motives and actions is no more evident than natural regularities like satiety after food or vegetation after planting. Most Mu'tazila admit these occur by habit (عادة) not necessity. Similarly, actions follow motives by habit, not necessity. 

Third argument: The author proposes an alternative: an action could be necessarily occurring at a specific time by its own essence, without any external cause. This is plausible according to Mu'tazila principles because they hold that accidents have specific times of occurrence with no cause for that timing. Similarly, the necessity of an action at a particular time could be intrinsic, not due to a cause. This undermines the need for a divine agent necessitating through motives. The author notes he has elaborated this in another work.

جبر
تكليف
قبيح

Key Takeaway
The first argument forces opponents into a dilemma: either accept compulsion or admit their proof is unsound.

The Habit Analogy: Second Argument Against Necessity of Actions
The author denies the necessity of actions from motives and uses an analogy with natural phenomena to argue for habitual, not necessary, connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the concept of habit (عادة) to deny the necessity of actions from motives, drawing an analogy with natural phenomena.

Core Idea
Actions follow motives by habit, not by necessity, just as satiety follows food.

In this lesson, we explore the second argument where the author directly denies the premise that actions are necessary when motives are present. He draws an analogy with well-known natural regularities like satiety after eating, quenching after drinking, and plant growth after sowing. Since most Mu'tazila concede these occur by habit (عادة) rather than necessity, the same should apply to actions and motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the denial of necessity
Notice the author begins with 'لا نسلم' (we do not concede), directly rejecting the opponent's claim that actions are necessarily linked to motives.
This is a standard dialectical move to challenge a premise.
2
Understand the analogies
The author lists three natural phenomena: satiety after food, quenching after drink, vegetation after planting. These are universally accepted as regular patterns.
Think about whether these are logically necessary or only empirically constant.
3
Connect to Mu'tazili view
The author notes that most Mu'tazila agree these natural events occur by habit, not necessity. So by their own principles, actions should also be habitual.
This leverages the opponents' own framework against them.
4
Apply the conclusion
The analogy concludes that actions following motives are also by habit, not necessity. Thus the opponent cannot claim necessity.
Remember that habit implies regularity but not logical or metaphysical compulsion.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second argument: The author denies the premise that actions are necessary given motives. He draws an analogy: the relation between motives and actions is no more evident than natural regularities like satiety after food or vegetation after planting. Most Mu'tazila admit these occur by habit (عادة) not necessity. Similarly, actions follow motives by habit, not necessity. 

Third argument: The author proposes an alternative: an action could be necessarily occurring at a specific time by its own essence, without any external cause. This is plausible according to Mu'tazila principles because they hold that accidents have specific times of occurrence with no cause for that timing. Similarly, the necessity of an action at a particular time could be intrinsic, not due to a cause. This undermines the need for a divine agent necessitating through motives. The author notes he has elaborated this in another work.

Key Terms
عادة
وجوب
دواعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author denies causality entirely, arguing that actions occur randomly.
Correction: The author does not deny causality; he only denies that the causal link is necessary. He upholds habitual regularity (عادة), which is a form of causation but without metaphysical compulsion.

Checkpoint
What do most Mu'tazila think about the occurrence of satiety after eating?
A) It is necessary by logical necessity.
B) It occurs by habit (عادة).
C) It is purely random.
D) It is caused by divine intervention directly.

Key Takeaway
Actions follow motives by habit, not necessity, as shown by analogy with common natural phenomena accepted as habitual by the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a strong desire to sin, do you tell yourself 'I couldn't help it'? After learning that motives lead to actions by habit, not necessity, will you stop excusing yourself and take full responsibility for your choices? What will you do the next time a powerful motive arises?
?
The author says natural events like satiety after eating are habitual, not necessary. Do you truly believe that God could break those habits at any moment? How does that belief affect the way you face difficulties or unexpected events? Are you ready to trust that God's decree overrides all habits?
?
If your motives are not necessary causes, then your habits of action are not fixed. Which habit of disobedience are you willing to break, starting today, by remembering that the connection is only habitual and not forced? What concrete step will you take?

Inherent Necessity: Third Argument on Time-Specific Actions
The author proposes that actions could be necessarily occurring at specific times by their own essence, consistent with Mu'tazili principles on accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proposes that an action could be necessarily occurring at a specific time by its own essence, without any external cause, which is plausible within Mu'tazili principles.

Core Idea
Actions may be intrinsically necessary at one time but impossible at others.

In this final argument, the author proposes an alternative explanation: an action might be necessarily occurring at a particular time by its very essence, and impossible at other times by its essence. This may seem far-fetched, but the author argues it is consistent with Mu'tazili principles, since they accept that accidents have specific times of occurrence with no cause for that timing. If timing can be intrinsic, then the necessity of an action at a specific time could also be intrinsic, undermining the need for a divine agent necessitating through motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the proposal
Notice the author asks 'لم لا يجوز' (why is it not permissible) suggesting that this is a logical possibility within the opponents' framework.
This is a typical kalam move to show that alternative explanations are possible.
2
Understand the analogy with accidents
The author references the Mu'tazili view that accidents (الأعراض) occur at specific times without a cause for that timing.
This is a known doctrine: accidents are 'temporalized' inherently.
3
Apply the reasoning to actions
If accidents can have time-specific existence without cause, then actions could similarly have time-specific necessity without an external necessitator.
This bypasses the need for motives as necessitating causes.
4
Consider the implication
If the action's necessity is intrinsic to that time, then the servant's power is not the agent; rather, the action occurs by its own essence.
This refutes the opponents' claim that actions are necessarily tied to motives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما احتجوا به أولاً من وجوه : الأول : إنكم إما أن تدعوا وجوب حصول الفعل عند حصول الداعي ، وامتناع حصول الفعل عند عدم تلك الدواعي . وإما أن لا تدعوا ذلك . فإن ادعيتم الأول لزمكم الجبر ، لأن حصول تلك الدواعي ليست من العبد . وإلا لزم التسلسل ، بل تكون من الله تعالى . وعلى هذا التقدير : إذا خلق الله في العبد تلك الدواعي ، وجب حصول ذلك الفعل ، شاء العبد أم أبى [ وإذا لم يخلقها فيه امتنع حصول الفعل ، شاء العبد أم أبي ] وإذا كان كذلك ، كان تكليف العبد تكليفاً بما ليس في وسعه. وأيضاً : فلأنه تعالى لما كان هو الفاعل لما يوجب حصول هذه الأفعال القبيحة ، كان أيضاً فاعلاً لهذه القبائح . لأنه لا فرق بين فعل القبيح ، وبين ما يوجب فعل القبيح ، أو فعل ما يجب عنده القبيح . فأما أن لا تقولوا بوجوب حصول الفعل عند حصول الإرادة ، وامتناع حصوله عند عدم الإرادة . فحينئذ لا يتم لكم هذا الاستدلال . لأنه إذا لم يجب حصول الفعل عند حصول الدواعي ، لم يلزم من عدم حصول الفعل عند توفر الدواعي ، ومن حصوله عند خلوص الصوارف : محال . وحينئذ يفسد هذا الدليل بالكلية . الثاني : أن نقول : لا نسلم أن حصول هذه الأفعال عند توفر الدواعي واجب . وذلك لأنه ليس حصول الأفعال عند حصول الدواعي أظهر من حصول الشبع للحي السليم ، عند تناول الغذاء الجيد . وحصول الري عقيب الشرب، وحصول النبت عند إلقاء البذر في الأرض الحرة ، مع حصول سائر الشرائط من السقي والشمس. ثم إن الأكثرين من المعتزلة : سلموا أن حصول هذه الأشياء عقيب هذه الأمور على سبيل العادة ، لا على سبيل الوجوب . فكذا ههنا . الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ذلك الفعل كان واجب الحدوث في ذلك الوقت بعينه لذاته، وكان ممتنع الحدوث في سائر الأوقات لعينه ولـذاته ؟ وهذا غير مستبعد على أصول المعتزلة . وذلك لأن عندهم الأعراض التي لا تبقى ، يختص حدوث كل واحد منها بوقت معين ، مع أنه كان يمتنع حدوثه قبل ذلك أو بعده . فإذا عقل اختصاص ذلك الجواز بوقت دون وقت ، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يختص وجوب حدوث هذه الأفعال ، بوقت دون وقت ، لا لمؤثر أصلا البتة ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير، يمتنع أن يقال : إن ذلك الفاعل إنما وقع بقدرة العبد . وعلى هذه الشبهة أصول كثيرة ، شرحناها في كتاب نهاية العقول .
And the answer to what they argued first is in aspects: First: Either you claim the necessity of the action occurring when the motive occurs, and the impossibility of the action occurring in the absence of those motives. Or you do not claim that. If you claim the first, compulsion (jabr) is necessary for you, because the occurrence of those motives is not from the servant. Otherwise, infinite regress would be necessary; rather, they are from God, Most High. On this assumption: if God creates those motives in the servant, the occurrence of that action becomes necessary, whether the servant wills or refuses [and if He does not create them in him, the occurrence of the action is impossible, whether the servant wills or refuses]. And if it is like that, then charging the servant is charging him with what is beyond his capacity. Also: because He, Most High, since He is the agent of what necessitates the occurrence of these evil actions, He is also the agent of these evil deeds. For there is no difference between doing the evil and what necessitates the evil, or doing that upon which the evil necessarily follows. As for you not saying that the action is necessary upon the occurrence of the will, and impossible upon the absence of the will—then this argument is not established for you. Because if the action is not necessary upon the occurrence of the motives, it does not follow from the non-occurrence of the action when motives are complete and its occurrence when hindrances are absent: an impossibility. And then this proof is invalidated entirely. Second: We say: we do not concede that the occurrence of these actions when motives are complete is necessary. That is because the occurrence of actions upon the occurrence of motives is not more evident than the occurrence of satiety for a healthy living being upon consuming good food, the occurrence of quenching after drinking, and the occurrence of vegetation upon casting seed into fertile soil with the remaining conditions of watering and sunlight. Then most of the Mu'tazila conceded that the occurrence of these things after these matters is by way of habit, not by way of necessity. So too here. Third: Why is it not permissible to say: That action was necessarily occurring at that specific time in itself, and was impossible of occurrence in other times in itself and by its essence? This is not far-fetched according to the principles of the Mu'tazila. That is because according to them, accidents that do not subsist, the occurrence of each one is restricted to a specific time, even though its occurrence before or after that is impossible. So if the restriction of that permissibility to a time and not another is understood, why is it not also permissible that the necessity of the occurrence of these actions is restricted to a time and not another, without any effect at all? On this assumption, it is impossible to say: That agent indeed occurred by the servant's power. And on this specious argument are many principles which we have explained in the book 'Nihayat al-Uqul'.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation of the first objection raised by opponents (likely the Mu'tazila) who argue that actions are necessitated by motives (دواعٍ). The author presents three arguments (وجوه) in response. 

First argument: The author forces a dilemma on the opponents. Either they claim that when motives are present the action necessarily occurs, and in their absence it is impossible, or they do not claim this. If they claim the first, they must accept compulsion (جبر) because the motives are not within the servant's power—they come from God. If God creates the motives, the action occurs necessarily whether the servant wills or not; if not, it is impossible. This makes divine obligation (تكليف) impossible, as it demands what is beyond one's capacity. Moreover, if God is the agent of what necessitates evil acts, He is effectively the agent of evil itself, since causing what necessitates evil is akin to doing evil. If the opponents do not claim necessity, their argument collapses because without necessity they cannot prove their point. 

Second argument: The author denies the premise that actions are necessary given motives. He draws an analogy: the relation between motives and actions is no more evident than natural regularities like satiety after food or vegetation after planting. Most Mu'tazila admit these occur by habit (عادة) not necessity. Similarly, actions follow motives by habit, not necessity. 

Third argument: The author proposes an alternative: an action could be necessarily occurring at a specific time by its own essence, without any external cause. This is plausible according to Mu'tazila principles because they hold that accidents have specific times of occurrence with no cause for that timing. Similarly, the necessity of an action at a particular time could be intrinsic, not due to a cause. This undermines the need for a divine agent necessitating through motives. The author notes he has elaborated this in another work.

Key Terms
وجوب
ذات
أعراض
وقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The actions are caused by God's direct will at each time.
B) Accidents in Mu'tazili thought have time-specific occurrence without a cause.
C) The servant's power determines the time of action.
D) Motives necessitate actions at specific times.

Key Takeaway
The author proposes that actions may be intrinsically necessary at one time but not others, consistent with Mu'tazili principles, offering an alternative to motive-based determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If an action's necessity could be intrinsic to the time itself, what does that imply about your own sense of control over your choices? In which area of your life do you most resist the idea that your actions are not entirely your own?
?
The Mu'tazili view reduces your actions to time-bound accidents. Does your daily reliance on Allah reflect a heart that truly believes every moment of obedience or sin is within His decree, or do you live as if your decisions are self-generated?
?
Consider a sin you have committed. If that action was 'necessarily occurring at that time' by its essence, how does that reshape your understanding of repentance? Are you truly accountable, or is it a matter of time? Let this question drive you to seek Allah's forgiveness while affirming your responsibility.

Refutation Using God's Knowledge
First sub-argument using God's knowledge to show necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari author uses God's universal knowledge to show that the opponent's objection leads to the same consequence of compulsion.

Core Idea
God's knowledge necessitates actions just as much as creation does.

The opponent claims that if God creates actions, the servant loses ability. The author counters by pointing out that God's own attribute of knowledge leads to the same determination. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the first demonstration focusing on God's knowledge.
2
Identify the logical move
The author shows that if God knows something will happen, it is impossible otherwise. This necessitates actions.
3
Connect to the objection
See how this mirrors the opponent's own criticism of Ash'ari creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : وهي قوله : « لو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لزم أن لا يكون متمكناً من الفعل والترك ) . فالجواب عنها : أن نقول : هذا أيضاً لازم عليكم في صور كثيرة : أولها : إن علم الله متعلق بجميع الجزئيات . وما علم أنه يقع : محال أن يقع ، وما علم أنه لا يقع : محال أن يقع . فيلزمهم في العلم ، كل ما ألزموه علينا في الإيجاد . الثاني : إنه تعالى أخبر عن أبي جهل ، أنه لا يؤمن ، فلو صدر عنه الإيمان ، انقلب الخبر الصدق : كذبا . وذلك محال . والمفضي إلى المحال : محال . فلزمهم في الجبر ما ألزموه علينا . الثالث : أن يقال : كلف أبو لهب بالإيمان . ومن الإيمان تصديق الله في كل ما أخبر عنه . ومما أخبر عنه : أنه لا يؤمن . فقد صار مكلفاً بأن يؤمن بأنه لا يؤمن .
And as for the second specious argument: which is his statement: 'If the servant is not the creator of his own actions, it necessarily follows that he is not capable of action and refraining.' The answer to it is: we say: This also is necessitated upon you in many cases: First: God's knowledge encompasses all particulars. What He knows will occur is impossible not to occur, and what He knows will not occur is impossible to occur. So all that they necessitated upon us regarding creation (ijad) is necessitated upon them regarding knowledge. Second: He, Most High, informed about Abu Jahl that he will not believe. So if faith were to issue from him, the true report would become a lie. That is impossible. And that which leads to the impossible is impossible. So they are bound by compulsion (jabr) just as they bound us. Third: It is said: Abu Lahab was obligated to believe. And part of belief is affirming God in everything He has informed about. And among what He informed about is that he will not believe. So he became obligated to believe that he will not believe.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of a second objection. The opponents argued: if the servant does not produce (يوجد) his own actions, then he cannot be capable of action and refraining (فعل وترك). The author responds by showing that the same consequence (loss of genuine capacity) follows from the opponents' own doctrines in three ways. 

First, using God's knowledge: God knows all particulars. What He knows will occur is impossible not to occur, and what He knows will not occur is impossible to occur. Thus, just as the opponents claim that if God creates actions, the servant is compelled, the same compulsion follows from God's knowledge because it necessitates the occurrence of what is known. So the opponents cannot escape compulsion by denying that the servant creates his acts. 

Second, using God's information: God informed that Abu Jahl will not believe. If Abu Jahl were to believe, God's truthful report would become false, which is impossible. Hence, it is impossible for Abu Jahl to believe, and he is constrained not to believe. Again, compulsion follows. 

Third, using the paradox of obligating Abu Lahab to believe: Abu Lahab was commanded to believe, and part of belief is affirming God's reports. God reported that Abu Lahab will not believe. Therefore, Abu Lahab is required to believe that he will not believe, which is self-contradictory. This shows the opponents' position leads to absurdity, and they cannot object to the Ash'ari view without facing similar or worse problems.

Key Terms
علم
إيجاد
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine knowledge does not affect human freedom.
Correction: The passage shows that if God's knowledge is infallible, then what He knows is necessitated, which implies a form of determinism.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what follows from God's knowledge of a future event?
A) The event is possible but not necessary
B) The event is impossible
C) The event must occur as known
D) The event is irrelevant to compulsion

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge necessitates actions, so the opponent's objection against divine creation applies equally to divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God knows every action you will take today. Does that make you feel helpless, or does it drive you to align your will with His? What choice will you make in the next hour knowing it is already known?
?
If nothing you do escapes His knowledge, what secret sin are you hiding as if it won't be brought to account? Will you repent now, or wait until the knowledge becomes a witness against you?
?
When you make plans, do you acknowledge that God's knowledge overrides your intentions? How will you surrender your next decision to His foreknowledge, even if it fails?

Refutation Using God's Report about Abu Jahl
Second sub-argument using God's report to show necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses God's true report about Abu Jahl to argue that the opponent's position leads to compulsion.

Core Idea
A true report from God makes the reported event necessary.

The second argument focuses on God's information. God informed that Abu Jahl would not believe. If he could believe, God's report would be false – an impossibility. Hence, Abu Jahl is compelled not to believe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second argument about Abu Jahl.
2
Identify the contradiction
If Abu Jahl believed, God's report would become false, which is impossible.
3
Apply the compulsion
Thus, it is impossible for Abu Jahl to believe – he is compelled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Correction: The argument shows that if God's report is true, then the event is fixed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's report about Abu Jahl is true and necessitates his unbelief. Reflect on a time when you doubted a clear promise or warning from God because it seemed unlikely to happen in your life. Did that doubt imply that you considered God's report possibly false? What will you do today to strengthen your trust in God's reports, even when your circumstances suggest otherwise?
?
The argument shows that compulsion (jabr) arises from God's truthfulness. In your own life, do you sometimes act as if your future is completely in your hands, ignoring that God has already decreed and reported some of your deeds? How can you balance your sense of responsibility with the reality that God's decree is certain? Name one specific attitude you will change.
?
Abu Jahl's unbelief is made necessary by God's true report. Consider your own salvation: God has reported in the Quran that some will be guided and some will be led astray. Are you complacent, thinking that your own efforts alone determine your fate? Or do you despair, thinking God's decree leaves no room for your striving? How does this lesson challenge you to respond to God's decree with both trust and action?

The Paradox of Obligating Abu Lahab
Third sub-argument showing a paradox in obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical paradox that arises from obligating Abu Lahab to believe while God informed he would not.

Core Idea
Obligation can conflict with divine report, exposing absurdity.

The third argument presents a theological paradox. Abu Lahab was commanded to believe, but part of belief is affirming God's reports, including the report that he will not believe. Thus, he is required to believe that he will not believe – a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the third argument about Abu Lahab.
2
Identify the elements
Obligation to believe, and belief includes affirming God's reports.
3
Spot the contradiction
He must believe that he will not believe – contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : وهي قوله : « لو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لزم أن لا يكون متمكناً من الفعل والترك ) . فالجواب عنها : أن نقول : هذا أيضاً لازم عليكم في صور كثيرة : أولها : إن علم الله متعلق بجميع الجزئيات . وما علم أنه يقع : محال أن يقع ، وما علم أنه لا يقع : محال أن يقع . فيلزمهم في العلم ، كل ما ألزموه علينا في الإيجاد . الثاني : إنه تعالى أخبر عن أبي جهل ، أنه لا يؤمن ، فلو صدر عنه الإيمان ، انقلب الخبر الصدق : كذبا . وذلك محال . والمفضي إلى المحال : محال . فلزمهم في الجبر ما ألزموه علينا . الثالث : أن يقال : كلف أبو لهب بالإيمان . ومن الإيمان تصديق الله في كل ما أخبر عنه . ومما أخبر عنه : أنه لا يؤمن . فقد صار مكلفاً بأن يؤمن بأنه لا يؤمن .
And as for the second specious argument: which is his statement: 'If the servant is not the creator of his own actions, it necessarily follows that he is not capable of action and refraining.' The answer to it is: we say: This also is necessitated upon you in many cases: First: God's knowledge encompasses all particulars. What He knows will occur is impossible not to occur, and what He knows will not occur is impossible to occur. So all that they necessitated upon us regarding creation (ijad) is necessitated upon them regarding knowledge. Second: He, Most High, informed about Abu Jahl that he will not believe. So if faith were to issue from him, the true report would become a lie. That is impossible. And that which leads to the impossible is impossible. So they are bound by compulsion (jabr) just as they bound us. Third: It is said: Abu Lahab was obligated to believe. And part of belief is affirming God in everything He has informed about. And among what He informed about is that he will not believe. So he became obligated to believe that he will not believe.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of a second objection. The opponents argued: if the servant does not produce (يوجد) his own actions, then he cannot be capable of action and refraining (فعل وترك). The author responds by showing that the same consequence (loss of genuine capacity) follows from the opponents' own doctrines in three ways. 

First, using God's knowledge: God knows all particulars. What He knows will occur is impossible not to occur, and what He knows will not occur is impossible to occur. Thus, just as the opponents claim that if God creates actions, the servant is compelled, the same compulsion follows from God's knowledge because it necessitates the occurrence of what is known. So the opponents cannot escape compulsion by denying that the servant creates his acts. 

Second, using God's information: God informed that Abu Jahl will not believe. If Abu Jahl were to believe, God's truthful report would become false, which is impossible. Hence, it is impossible for Abu Jahl to believe, and he is constrained not to believe. Again, compulsion follows. 

Third, using the paradox of obligating Abu Lahab to believe: Abu Lahab was commanded to believe, and part of belief is affirming God's reports. God reported that Abu Lahab will not believe. Therefore, Abu Lahab is required to believe that he will not believe, which is self-contradictory. This shows the opponents' position leads to absurdity, and they cannot object to the Ash'ari view without facing similar or worse problems.

Key Terms
تكليف
إيمان
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine obligation and divine foreknowledge can coexist without contradiction.
Correction: This argument shows that if both are taken literally, a contradiction arises.

Checkpoint
What contradictory obligation arises for Abu Lahab?
A) To believe in God
B) To believe that he will not believe
C) To disbelieve
D) To change God's report

Key Takeaway
The obligation to believe combined with God's report creates a paradox, undermining the opponent's objection.

When you read that Abu Lahab was commanded to believe he would not believe, do you feel a similar tension in your own life: God commands you to do something, yet His decree seems to make it impossible? Will you let that tension drive you to despair or to deeper reliance on His mercy?
?
This paradox exposes the absurdity of separating God's command from His report. Where in your life are you acting as if God's command is unreal because of what you think you know about your own future? Will you today choose to obey His command despite your fears?

Al-Razi’s Third Objection: Timing of Actions and Accidents
Al-Razi presents a third objection using Mu'tazilite accident theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the Mu'tazilite doctrine of non-persisting accidents to construct a dialectical objection against their own view on human action.

Core Idea
Al-Razi turns Mu'tazilite premises against them: if accidents can be time-specific, so can action-necessity.

Al-Razi presents a third objection to the Mu'tazilite position that human actions are created by the servant. He cleverly exploits their own theory of accidents—which holds that each accident occurs at a specific time and cannot occur elsewhere—and asks why the same logic would not apply to the necessity of actions. This forces the Mu'tazilite into a dilemma: either accept that action-necessity is time-specific without a cause, which undermines human power, or give up their accident doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice how al-Razi frames this as a question.
This is a dialectical objection, not a final position.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite premise
Find where al-Razi states the Mu'tazilite view on accidents. They hold that accidents occur at specific times and cannot occur before or after.
This premise is the foundation of the objection.
3
Trace the analogy from possibility to necessity
See how al-Razi argues: if possibility (jawāz) can be time-specific, then why can't necessity (wujūb) also be time-specific without a cause?
The key move is extending the Mu'tazilite logic.
4
Understand the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that on this basis, the action cannot be attributed to the servant's power. This undermines the Mu'tazilite view of human agency.
The objection ends with a reference to a fuller treatment elsewhere.
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasped the main premise used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ذلك الفعل كان واجب الحدوث في ذلك الوقت بعينه لذاته، وكان ممتنع الحدوث في سائر الأوقات لعينه ولذاته ؟ وهذا غير مستبعد على أصول المعتزلة . وذلك لأن عندهم الأعراض التي لا تبقى ، يختص حدوث كل واحد منها بوقت معين ، مع أنه كان يمتنع حدوثه قبل ذلك أو بعده . فإذا عقل اختصاص ذلك الجواز بوقت دون وقت ، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يختص وجوب حدوث هذه الأفعال ، بوقت دون وقت ، لا لمؤثر أصلا البتة ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير، يمتنع أن يقال : إن ذلك الفاعل إنما وقع بقدرة العبد . وعلى هذه الشبهة أصول كثيرة ، شرحناها في كتاب نهاية العقول .
The third: Why is it not permissible to say: That action was necessarily occurring at that exact time for its own essence, and was impossible to occur at all other times for its own essence and for its own essence? And this is not far-fetched according to the principles of the Mu'tazila. That is because according to them, the accidents that do not persist, the occurrence of each one of them is specific to a particular time, while its occurrence before or after that was impossible. So if the specification of that possibility (jawāz) to a time rather than another is understood, then why is it not also permissible that the necessity (wujūb) of occurrence of these actions be specific to a time rather than another, with no cause (mu'aththir) at all? And on this estimation, it is impossible to say that that agent occurred by the power of the servant. And concerning this objection (shubha) there are many principles, which we have explained in the book Nihāyat al-‘Uqūl.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection (al-thāliṯ) raised by al-Rāzī against the Mu'tazilite view that human actions are created by the servant. It is a rhetorical argument that exploits the Mu'tazilites' own doctrine of accidents (a‘rāḍ). Al-Rāzī begins by asking: Why is it not permissible to claim that a particular action is necessarily occurring (wājib al-ḥudūṯ) at its exact time for its own essence (li-ḏātihi), and impossible for its own essence at any other time? He then argues that this is not implausible given Mu'tazilite principles. According to the Mu'tazila, accidents (a‘rāḍ) are entities that do not persist over time; each accident occurs at a specific moment and cannot occur before or after. They accept that the possibility (jawāz) of an accident is restricted to one time. Al-Rāzī then makes the key move: if possibility can be time-specific, then why not also necessity (wujūb)? That is, an action could be necessarily occurring at one time and necessarily not occurring at others, without any external cause (lā li-mu'aththir aṣlan al-batta). On this basis, it becomes impossible to attribute the action to the servant's power (qudrat al-‘abd). The passage concludes by noting that the full refutation of this objection is lengthy and has been covered in his book Nihāyat al-‘Uqūl. The argument is purely dialectical: it does not assert the necessity view as true but shows that the Mu'tazilites cannot consistently deny it given their own premises.

Key Terms
شبهة
أعراض
جواز
وجوب
مؤثر
قدرة العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi is endorsing the view that actions are necessary by their own essence.
Correction: The passage is an objection (shubha) to the Mu'tazilites, not al-Razi's own final position; it shows a contradiction in their system.

Checkpoint
What premise from the Mu'tazilites does al-Rāzī use to build his objection?
A) That actions are eternal
B) That accidents have specific times of occurrence
C) That God creates all actions
D) That the servant creates his own actions

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses the Mu'tazilites' own doctrine of time-specific accidents to argue that actions, too, could be time-specific in necessity, thereby undermining the servant's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In what area of your life are you most attached to the idea that your actions are entirely your own, even though the premises of your worldview might require you to acknowledge they are necessitated? Would you be willing to re-examine that attachment in light of this argument?
?
This passage shows that logic can lead to conclusions that undermine human responsibility if starting premises are wrong. Are you willing to examine the foundations of your own beliefs about divine decree and human power with the same intellectual rigor, even if it makes you uncomfortable?
?
When you read that 'on this estimation, it is impossible to say that that agent occurred by the power of the servant', do you feel a sense of relief or anxiety? What does your reaction reveal about your deepest assumptions about your own agency and accountability before Allah?

The Tenth Argument: Commanding the Unknown is Impossible
Sanusi presents the tenth argument and concludes the series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tenth argument against the Mu'tazilite position on capacity, which demonstrates that commanding an action with unknown components is a command to do the impossible.

Core Idea
Even a simple finger movement involves unknown parts, spaces, and moments—commanding it is commanding the impossible.

This passage presents the tenth and final argument in a series of proofs against the Mu'tazilite claim that God does not command what is beyond human capacity. The author analyzes the simple act of moving a finger to show that its complexity is unknown to the agent, making the command a command to do what is impossible. This argument seals the case against the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the careful breakdown of a finger movement into parts, spaces, and moments.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The argument begins with a command to move a finger, then shows that this implies a command to move all its constituent parts through all spaces and moments. Identify each step in the reasoning.
The author uses 'then' (ثم) to connect successive implications.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Understand how the unknowability of these components makes the command a command to bring about the unknown, which is impossible. Then see how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.
The final sentence generalizes to all ten arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : إن العبد إذا صار مأموراً ، بأن يحرك أصبع نفسه ، فقد صار مأمورا بتحريك جملة الأجزاء التي منها تركب ذلك الإصبع ، في جميع تلك الأحياز، التي منها تركبت تلك المسافة في جميع تلك الآنات ، التي منها تركب ذلك الزمان . ثم أن كمية تلك الأجزاء مجهولة ، وكمية تلك الأحياز مجهولة ، وكمية تلك الآنات مجهولة . فكان الأمر بهذا الفعل ، أمراً بتكوين ما هو مجهول من هذه الوجوه . وذلك تكليف بما ليس في الوسع . لأن القصد إلى تكوين ما هو غير معلوم ، ولا متصور : تكليف بما لا يطاق
فثبت بهذه الوجوه العشرة : أن الذي الزموه علينا ، فهو لازم عليهم . وهذه المسائل العشرة ، كل ما هو جوابهم عنها ، هو جوابنا عما الزمـوه علينا
The tenth: If the servant is commanded to move his own finger, then he becomes commanded to move all the parts from which that finger is composed, in all those spaces from which that distance is composed, in all those moments from which that time is composed. Then the quantity of those parts is unknown, the quantity of those spaces is unknown, and the quantity of those moments is unknown. So the command to do this act is a command to bring about what is unknown in these respects. And that is a command to what is not within capacity. Because the intention to bring about what is not known nor conceived is a command to what cannot be borne. Thus it is established by these ten proofs that what they obligated upon us is obligated upon them. And these ten issues, whatever their answer to them, is our answer to what they obligated upon us.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth and final argument in a series of proofs against the Mu'tazilite claim that God does not command what is beyond human capacity. The argument proceeds by analyzing a simple action: moving one's finger. The author demonstrates that even this seemingly simple action involves an immense complexity of unknown parts, spaces, and moments. Commanding such an action requires commanding the creation of something whose details are unknown to the agent, which constitutes a command to do what is impossible. The conclusion then generalizes: all ten arguments together show that the Mu'tazilites' own position (that God never commands the impossible) is actually refuted by their own reasoning, and their objections to the Ash'ari position are parallel to the Ash'ari responses.

Key Terms

قدرة
إصبع
أجزاء
أحياز
آنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A simple action like moving a finger is fully known and within human capacity, so commanding it is not commanding the impossible.
Correction: The finger's movement involves countless unknown parts, spaces, and moments. Since these are unknown, the command is to bring about something unknowable, which is impossible.

Checkpoint

B) Commanding an action with unknown components is a command to do the impossible
C) God only commands what is within capacity
D) The Mu'tazilites agree with the Ash'aris

Key Takeaway
The tenth argument demonstrates that even a seemingly simple action involves unknown complexity, making its command a command to do the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The First Objection: Essences, States, and the Impossibility of Divine Command
First Ash'ari objection against Mu'tazilite view on human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari objection that Mu'tazilite concepts of essences and states lead to the conclusion that commanding human action is commanding the impossible.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris argue that if essences exist in non-existence, power can never affect them.

In their debate with the Mu'tazilites over human capacity, the Ash'aris raised a series of objections. The first challenges the Mu'tazilite view that essences (dhawāt) exist prior to creation and that the attribute of existence is a state (ḥāl). This lesson unpacks that objection and shows how it targets the logical coherence of divine command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection once, then its translation. Notice the three components: essences, attribute of existence as a state, and the existing quiddity.
The passage uses technical kalam terms; focus on their definitions.
2
Analyze each component
For each component, identify why the Ash'ari claims it cannot be an object of power. Why are essences in non-existence not subject to power? Why is a state not an object of power? Why is the existing quiddity not subject?
The argument is that none of these are fit to be 'mamdūr' (object of power).
3
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that the command to act becomes a command to do the impossible (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). Grasp how the combination is also impossible.
4
Consider the exception
Note that the author exempts Abu al-Husayn al-Basri because he denies that non-existents are things. This shows the objection is specific to certain Mu'tazilite views.
The exception is crucial for understanding the target of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أصحابنا . فقد أوردوا عليهم الزامات أخرى :
أحدها: أن الذوات عندهم حاصلة في العدم، فلا يمكن أن يكون للقدر أثر فيها . وأما الصفة صفة (۲) الوجود فهي عندهم حال ، والحال لا تكون معلومة ولا مقدورة . وأما الماهية الموجودة فهي ليست إلا الماهية وإلا الوجود . ولما لم يكن واحد منهما صالحا للمقدورية ، لم يكن المجموع أيضاً صالحاً للمقدورية . فيلزمهم أن يكون التكليف بالفعل ، تكليفاً بما لا يطاق .
إلا أن هذا الإلزام غير وارد على ( أبي الحسين ، فإنه ينكر كون المعدوم شيئاً .
وثانيها : إن عندهم الكائنية معللة بمعنى يوجبها . ثم إن أكثر الخلق لا يتصورون هذا المعنى . والقدرة لا تأثير لها في الكائنية أصلا . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : التكليف إن وقع بتحصيل الكائنية ، كان ذلك تكليفا بما لا يطاق لان العبد لا قدرة له على الكائنية . وإن وقع بتحصيل ذلك الموجب ، فذلك غير متصور الماهية ، عند أكثر الخلق . فكان التكليف بإيجاده تكليفاً بتحصيل ما لا تكون ماهيته متصورة عند الذهن . وذلك تكليف بما لا يطاق . لأن إيجاد الشيء لا يمكن إلا بالقصد إلى تحصيله والقصد إلى تحصيله مشروط بالعلم بماهيته . وعند فقدان هذا العلم يكون هذا تكليفاً بما لا يطاق . إلا أن هذا الإلزام غير وارد على ( أبي الحسين ، لأنه لا يثبت هذا المعنى . والله أعلم
And as for our companions, they have brought other objections against them: 
First: That essences (al-dhawāt) according to them exist in non-existence, so power cannot have an effect in them. As for the attribute of existence, it is according to them a state (ḥāl), and a state is neither known nor able to be an object of power. As for the existing quiddity (al-māhiyah al-mawjūdah), it is nothing but the quiddity and the existence. And since neither of them is suitable for being an object of power, then the combination also is not suitable. Hence it follows necessarily that the command to act is a command to what cannot be borne. 
Except that this objection does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn, for he denies that non-existence is a thing. 
Second: That according to them, the state of being (al-kā’iniyyah) is caused by a meaning (ma‘nā) that necessitates it. Then most of creation do not conceive this meaning. And power has no effect in the state of being at all. When this is established, we say: If the command is to attain the state of being, then that is a command to what cannot be borne because the servant has no power over the state of being. And if it is to attain that necessitating meaning, then that meaning is not conceivable in its quiddity for most of creation. So the command to bring it about is a command to attain something whose quiddity is not conceived by the mind. And that is a command to what cannot be borne. Because bringing a thing into existence is only possible by intending to attain it, and intending to attain it is conditioned on knowledge of its quiddity. And in the absence of this knowledge, this becomes a command to what cannot be borne. Except that this objection does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn, for he does not affirm this meaning. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents two objections raised by the Ash'arīs against the Mu'tazilite position on human action and capacity. The first objection focuses on the Mu'tazilite concept of essences existing in non-existence and the notion of states (aḥwāl). If essences exist in non-existence, power (qudra) cannot affect them because they are already 'there'. The attribute of existence is a state, which is neither known nor an object of power. The existing quiddity splits into quiddity and existence, neither of which alone is a proper subject of power. Therefore, no human action can be an object of power, leading to the conclusion that commanding the act is commanding the impossible. The author notes that this objection does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī, who denies that non-existents are things. The second objection concerns the Mu'tazilite concept of 'kā’iniyyah' (the state of being brought about), which they say is caused by a meaning. Since most people do not conceive this meaning, and power has no effect on the state of being itself, the command either aims at the state of being (impossible) or at the meaning (unconceived). In either case, it becomes a command to what is impossible, because one cannot intend to bring about something without knowledge of its quiddity. Again, this does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn, who does not affirm such a meaning.

Key Terms
ذوات (dhawāt)
صفة (ṣifah)
حال (ḥāl)
ماهية (māhiyah)
قدرة (qudra)
تكليف بما لا يطاق (taklif mā lā yuṭāq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection refutes all Mu'tazilites.
Correction: The author notes that it does not apply to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri who denies that non-existents are things.

Checkpoint
Why does the first objection not apply to Abu al-Husayn?
A) Because he denies states (ahwal)
B) Because he denies that non-existence is a thing
C) Because he believes essences exist in non-existence
D) Because he accepts the Ash'ari view

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that because essences, the attribute of existence (as a state), and the existing quiddity are all inaccessible to power, the command to act becomes impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to obey a command of Allah, do you ever blame your 'lack of capacity' as if it were a fixed essence? How does this lesson's analysis challenge you to instead examine your will and reliance on Allah? What specific step will you take today to act against that excuse?

In light of this lesson, what is one area where you have assumed powerlessness, and what action will you commit to this week to prove that Allah's power can transform it?

The objection exempts Abū al-Ḥusayn because he denies that non-existence is a thing. Is there a 'thing' in your life—a sin, a broken relationship, a bad habit—that you treat as a permanent 'non-existent' that cannot be changed? Will you instead treat it as something Allah can affect through your sincere effort and His power? What is the first concrete step you will take?

The Second Objection: The State of Being and the Unknowable Cause
Second Ash'ari objection based on Mu'tazilite notion of kā’iniyyah and its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that if actions are brought about by a meaning (ma'na) that most people cannot conceive, then commanding such actions is impossible.

Core Idea
If an action's very 'being-brought-about' depends on an unconceived meaning, the command is impossible.

The second objection targets the Mu'tazilite concept of 'kā’iniyyah' — the state of being brought about — which they claim is caused by a meaning. The Ash'aris argue that this leads to a dilemma: either the command is to attain the state of being (impossible) or to attain the meaning (unconceived), both of which are beyond human capacity. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the second objection and its translation. Note the terms: kā’iniyyah (state of being) and ma‘nā (meaning).
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
The Mu'tazilites claim that the becoming of an act (kā’iniyyah) is caused by a meaning (ma‘nā) that necessitates it. Most people do not conceive this meaning, and power has no effect on the state of being itself.
3
Follow the dilemma
Two possibilities: if the command is to attain the state of being, it is impossible because the servant has no power over it. If the command is to attain the meaning, that meaning is unconceived for most people, so it is impossible to intend it.
The reasoning is that intending requires knowledge of the quiddity.
4
Analyze the impossibility of the second branch
Bringing something into existence requires intending to attain it, and intending is conditioned on knowledge of its quiddity. Since the meaning is not conceived, the command to bring it about is impossible.
5
Note the exception
Again, this objection does not apply to Abu al-Husayn because he does not affirm such a meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أصحابنا . فقد أوردوا عليهم الزامات أخرى :
أحدها: أن الذوات عندهم حاصلة في العدم، فلا يمكن أن يكون للقدر أثر فيها . وأما الصفة صفة (۲) الوجود فهي عندهم حال ، والحال لا تكون معلومة ولا مقدورة . وأما الماهية الموجودة فهي ليست إلا الماهية وإلا الوجود . ولما لم يكن واحد منهما صالحا للمقدورية ، لم يكن المجموع أيضاً صالحاً للمقدورية . فيلزمهم أن يكون التكليف بالفعل ، تكليفاً بما لا يطاق .
إلا أن هذا الإلزام غير وارد على ( أبي الحسين ، فإنه ينكر كون المعدوم شيئاً .
وثانيها : إن عندهم الكائنية معللة بمعنى يوجبها . ثم إن أكثر الخلق لا يتصورون هذا المعنى . والقدرة لا تأثير لها في الكائنية أصلا . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : التكليف إن وقع بتحصيل الكائنية ، كان ذلك تكليفا بما لا يطاق لان العبد لا قدرة له على الكائنية . وإن وقع بتحصيل ذلك الموجب ، فذلك غير متصور الماهية ، عند أكثر الخلق . فكان التكليف بإيجاده تكليفاً بتحصيل ما لا تكون ماهيته متصورة عند الذهن . وذلك تكليف بما لا يطاق . لأن إيجاد الشيء لا يمكن إلا بالقصد إلى تحصيله والقصد إلى تحصيله مشروط بالعلم بماهيته . وعند فقدان هذا العلم يكون هذا تكليفاً بما لا يطاق . إلا أن هذا الإلزام غير وارد على ( أبي الحسين ، لأنه لا يثبت هذا المعنى . والله أعلم
And as for our companions, they have brought other objections against them: 
First: That essences (al-dhawāt) according to them exist in non-existence, so power cannot have an effect in them. As for the attribute of existence, it is according to them a state (ḥāl), and a state is neither known nor able to be an object of power. As for the existing quiddity (al-māhiyah al-mawjūdah), it is nothing but the quiddity and the existence. And since neither of them is suitable for being an object of power, then the combination also is not suitable. Hence it follows necessarily that the command to act is a command to what cannot be borne. 
Except that this objection does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn, for he denies that non-existence is a thing. 
Second: That according to them, the state of being (al-kā’iniyyah) is caused by a meaning (ma‘nā) that necessitates it. Then most of creation do not conceive this meaning. And power has no effect in the state of being at all. When this is established, we say: If the command is to attain the state of being, then that is a command to what cannot be borne because the servant has no power over the state of being. And if it is to attain that necessitating meaning, then that meaning is not conceivable in its quiddity for most of creation. So the command to bring it about is a command to attain something whose quiddity is not conceived by the mind. And that is a command to what cannot be borne. Because bringing a thing into existence is only possible by intending to attain it, and intending to attain it is conditioned on knowledge of its quiddity. And in the absence of this knowledge, this becomes a command to what cannot be borne. Except that this objection does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn, for he does not affirm this meaning. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents two objections raised by the Ash'arīs against the Mu'tazilite position on human action and capacity. The first objection focuses on the Mu'tazilite concept of essences existing in non-existence and the notion of states (aḥwāl). If essences exist in non-existence, power (qudra) cannot affect them because they are already 'there'. The attribute of existence is a state, which is neither known nor an object of power. The existing quiddity splits into quiddity and existence, neither of which alone is a proper subject of power. Therefore, no human action can be an object of power, leading to the conclusion that commanding the act is commanding the impossible. The author notes that this objection does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī, who denies that non-existents are things. The second objection concerns the Mu'tazilite concept of 'kā’iniyyah' (the state of being brought about), which they say is caused by a meaning. Since most people do not conceive this meaning, and power has no effect on the state of being itself, the command either aims at the state of being (impossible) or at the meaning (unconceived). In either case, it becomes a command to what is impossible, because one cannot intend to bring about something without knowledge of its quiddity. Again, this does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn, who does not affirm such a meaning.

Key Terms
كائنية (kā’iniyyah)
معنى (ma‘nā)
قدرة (qudra)
تكليف بما لا يطاق (taklif mā lā yuṭāq)
ماهية (māhiyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second objection refutes all Mu'tazilites.
Correction: The author notes that it does not apply to Abu al-Husayn because he does not affirm such a meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, why is the command to attain the state of being (kā’iniyyah) impossible?
A) Because the meaning is unconceived
B) Because the servant has no power over the state of being
C) Because the state of being is a state (ḥāl)
D) Because the command is to do the impossible

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that if actions require an unconceived cause, the divine command becomes a command to do the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held yourself or others to a standard based on a reason you could not clearly define? How does this passage expose the injustice of demanding something whose justification is unknown?
?
When you command your own soul to obey Allah, do you first seek to understand the 'quiddity' of what is required, or do you act blindly? How does this lesson call you to pursue knowledge before action?
?
The Ash'ari argues that an inconceivable cause makes a command impossible. In your life, have you ever dismissed a command because its reason seemed inconceivable? How does this passage challenge you to examine whether you are excusing yourself or genuinely facing an impossibility?
