The Proof from 'Lord of the Worlds' for Creation of Actions
First proof from رب العالمين that Allah is Creator of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic title 'Rabb al-'alameen' is used by Ahl al-Sunna to prove that Allah is the Creator of the actions of servants.

Core Idea
The Lord of the worlds must also be the Creator of all actions within the world.

The verse 'Rabb al-'alameen' (Lord of the worlds) is often invoked in discussions of predestination. The Mu'tazila used it to argue that all God's acts are beneficial, but the Ahl al-Sunna turned it into a proof that God creates all actions, including those of humans. Let's examine how this argument works and why it refutes both the Mu'tazila and the Jabriyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: Mu'tazila claim, then Sunni response.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila interpretation
Identify how the Mu'tazila define 'tarbiya' and how they use it to support their doctrine that all God's acts are for the benefit of creatures.
They contrast their view with that of the Jabriyya.
3
Analyze the Sunni counterargument
Focus on the first aspect: actions are part of the world, Lordship implies disposal, disposal implies creation. See the logical steps.
This is a deductive proof from the meaning of 'Rabb'.
4
Reflect on the concept of disposal (tasarruf)
Consider why 'disposal' of actions necessarily means creating them. How does this refute the Mu'tazila who claim servants create their own acts?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس
تيانا (يب العالمين)
قالت المعتزلة : التربية عبارة عن إيصال المنافع إلى الغير، لغرض الإحسان إليه . فلما وصف الله نفسه بأنه رب العالمين ، وجب أن يقال : إن كل ما أوصل الله إلى الغير فإنه منفعة ، وأنه تعالى إنما أوصلها إليهم لغرض الإحسان إليهم. وهذا إنما يتم على قولنا . فإنا نقول : إنه تعالى ما خلق الخلق ، وما كلف أحداً إلا لغرض الإحسان إليهم . فأما على قول الجبرية ،
وهو أنه تعالى خلقهم للنار والعذاب الدائم، فهذا لا يكون رباً ولا مربياً البتة
·
قال أهل السنة والجماعة : هذه الآية من أدل الدلائل على صحة قولنا .
وبيانه من وجوه :
الأول : إن العالم كل موجود سوى الله ، فتكون أفعال العباد من جملة العالم . ولما كان رباً للعالمين، وجب أن يكون رباً لأفعال العباد والرب هو المتصرف [ بالشيء (۱) ] فيلزم كونه متصرفاً في أفعال العباد ، والتصرف في أفعال العباد لا يكون إلا بخلقها وتكوينها . قدلت هذه الآية على أنه تعالى خالق لأفعال العباد
Chapter Five
Statement regarding 'Lord of the worlds'
The Mu'tazila said: 'Tarbiya (nurturing) is the conveyance of benefits to others for the purpose of kindness to them. So when Allah described Himself as 'Lord of the worlds', it must be said that everything that Allah conveys to others is a benefit, and that He the Exalted only conveys it to them for the purpose of kindness to them. And this is only consistent with our view. For we say: He the Exalted did not create the creation and did not charge anyone except for the purpose of kindness to them. But according to the view of the Jabriyya, which is that He the Exalted created them for the Fire and eternal punishment, then He would not be a lord or a nurturer at all.'
The Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: 'This verse is among the strongest evidences for the correctness of our view. And its explanation is from several aspects:
First: The world is every existent other than Allah, so the actions of servants are part of the world. Since He is Lord of the worlds, it is necessary that He be Lord of the actions of servants. And the Lord is the one who disposes of a thing, so it follows that He is disposer of the actions of servants, and disposal of the actions of servants is only by creating and originating them. So this verse indicates that He the Exalted is the Creator of the actions of servants.'
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite interpretation of the phrase 'Rabb al-'alameen' (Lord of the worlds). The author first presents the Mu'tazila claim: 'tarbiya' means conveying benefits for the purpose of kindness. Since Allah is described as 'Rabb', everything He provides must be a benefit intended for kindness. This, they argue, supports their view that Allah only creates and commands for the benefit of creatures, unlike the Jabriyya who hold that Allah creates some for eternal punishment.
The Ahl al-Sunna then respond: This very verse is a strong proof for the Sunni position. They explain it from several aspects, and here they give the first aspect: The world (al-'alam) is everything other than Allah. Thus, the actions of servants are part of the world. Since Allah is Lord of the worlds (Rabb al-'alameen), He must be Lord of the actions of servants. Lordship (rububiyya) implies disposal (tasarruf), and disposal over actions requires creating and originating them. Therefore, the verse proves that Allah is the Creator of the actions of servants, which is the Sunni doctrine against the Mu'tazilite view that servants create their own actions.

Key Terms
رب العالمين
التربية
المعتزلة
الجبرية
أهل السنة
أفعال العباد
التصرف
خلق أفعال العباد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'Rabb al-'alameen' only refers to lordship over substances, not including actions.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that actions are part of the world (al-alam includes all existents other than Allah), so Lordship extends to actions.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof from Ahl al-Sunna, why must Allah be the Creator of the actions of servants?
A) Because actions are part of the world and He is Lord of the world
B) Because the Mu'tazila cannot prove otherwise
C) Because the verse explicitly says so
D) Because the Jabriyya denied it

Key Takeaway
The title 'Lord of the worlds' implies that Allah is the Creator and Disposer of all actions, affirming the Sunni doctrine of creation of acts.

?
The Mu'tazila could not accept that Allah creates harm or difficulty. But this verse shows His lordship includes all actions. Is there a trial in your life that you resist because you think it contradicts His goodness? How can submitting to His disposal change your heart today?

Third Proof: Faith More Beneficial Than All Else
Ahl al-Sunna present a reductio argument about faith and lordship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument used by Ahl al-Sunna to refute the Mu'tazila claim that the servant creates his own faith.

Core Idea
If faith were self-created, the servant would be a better lord than God—an absurdity that refutes the premise.

This passage presents the third proof in a series by Ahl al-Sunna against the Mu'tazila doctrine that humans create their own actions. Here, the focus is on faith (iman): the argument shows that if faith were the servant's own creation, it would lead to the absurd conclusion that the servant is a more complete nurturer and lord than God Himself. This logical reduction is designed to uphold the Sunni position that all actions, including faith, are created by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the original Arabic and the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'لما كان', 'فوجب', 'وهذا يوجب'.
2
Identify the hypothetical premise
The argument begins with the premise that faith is from the servant's own creation ('خلقه'). This is the Mu'tazila position being tested.
3
Follow the reductio chain
Trace how this premise leads to the inference that the servant's nurturing is more complete than God's, and then to the conclusion that the servant is a better lord.
This is the absurd consequence that refutes the premise.
4
Understand the resolution
Recognize that the absurdity shows the premise is false, thus affirming that God alone is the true Lord and His self-praise is valid.
The final sentence is the correct Sunni position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الإيمان أكثر نفعاً للعبد من كل سواه ، ولما كان إيمان العبد من خلقه ، كان تربية العبد لنفسه أكمل من تربية الله له . فوجب أن يكون كون العبد رباً ومربياً لنفسه ، أكمل من كون الله ربا ومربياً له . وهذا يوجب أن يصح من الله التمدح بكونه رباً للعالمين والله أعلم
The third: That faith is more beneficial for the servant than everything else. And since the faith of the servant is from his own creation, then the servant's nurturing of himself is more complete than God's nurturing of him. So it is necessary that the servant being a lord and nurturer of himself is more complete than God being a lord and nurturer of him. And this requires that it be valid for God to praise Himself for being Lord of the worlds. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is the third point of a theological proof (likely from Ahl al-Sunna) that refutes the Mu'tazila position that the servant creates his own actions, specifically here regarding faith. The argument proceeds as a reductio ad absurdum: If faith is the servant's own creation (خلقه), then the servant's nurturing (تربية) of himself in faith is more complete than God's nurturing of him. This leads to the conclusion that the servant being a lord (رب) and nurturer of himself is more complete than God being so. But this is absurd because God is the true Lord. Therefore, the initial premise—that the servant creates his own faith—is false. The conclusion then affirms that it is valid for God to praise Himself as Lord of the worlds, which is the desired Sunni position. The phrase 'والله أعلم' (And God knows best) is a common closing.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الخلق
الرب
المربي
التمدح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this argument proves the servant's independence in creating faith.
Correction: The argument actually refutes that claim by showing it leads to the absurd consequence that the servant is a better lord than God, so the belief in self-creation is false.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence if the servant creates his own faith?
A) The servant would be more powerful than God.
B) The servant would be a better nurturer and lord than God.
C) The servant would be responsible for his own sin.
D) The servant would not need faith.

Key Takeaway
If faith were self-created, the servant would surpass God in lordship—an absurdity that proves all actions, including faith, are created by God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that even your faith—the very thing you think you 'have'—is not your own doing but a creation of God? How does that realization change the way you view your own religious achievements?
?
When you feel proud of your faith or your good deeds, does this argument puncture that pride? If God is the one who created your faith, then what do you have to boast about? How can you actively remind yourself of this daily?
?
If someone challenged you and said, 'But you are the one who chooses to believe,' what from this argument would you say to them? And more importantly, do you live in a way that shows you depend on God to give you faith, or do you act as if your faith is self-generated?

Mu'tazila Argument and First Sunni Proof
Mu'tazila argument and first Sunni proof (reductio).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument from the verse and the first Sunni proof that reduces it to absurdity by considering a lifelong disbeliever who repents at the last moment.

Core Idea
If God's ownership depended on human action, He could become 'owned' — an absurdity.

The Mu'tazila interpreted 'Owner of the Day of Judgment' to mean that God's recompense depends on human free will. The first Sunni response shows this leads to a logical contradiction: a man who disbelieves for eighty years and then believes for a moment would make God's ownership dependent on that moment, reducing God to a state worse than a slave. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'قيل: (مالك يوم الدين)' to 'فوجب أن يكون مالكاً ليوم الدين'. Understand that they link al-din (recompense) to human action, so God must be owner of that day only if humans have free will.
Notice the key term 'الدين' is interpreted as 'جزاء'.
2
Read the first Sunni proof
Read the first proof from 'قال أهل السنة والجماعة' to 'تعالى الله عنه علواً كبيراً'. Follow the reductio: imagine a man who disbelieves for 80 years then believes and dies. On Mu'tazila grounds, his brief faith would make God 'owned' — a clear absurdity.
The proof ends with a strong tanzih statement.
3
Analyze the reductio
Understand that the first proof is a reductio ad absurdum: it shows that the Mu'tazila position leads to consequences that are impossible for God, thus their premise must be false.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasped the core of the reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في
قيل: (مالك يوم الدين)
قالت المعتزلة : الدين هو الجزاء. والجزاء إنما يحسن على الفعل فإذا [ كان ] لا فعل للعبد ، امتنع ترتب الجزاء عليه . فوجب أن يكون مالكاً ليوم الدين .
قال أهل السنة والجماعة : كونه تعالى مالكاً ليوم الدين ، لا يتم إلا على قولنا . وبيانه من وجوه :
الأول : إنا نفرض رجلاً استمر على كفره ثمانين سنة ، ثم اتفق أن آمن ، ومات عقيب إيمانه بلحظة . فهذا الرجل عصى الله بالكفر في جميع عمره ، ثم أتى بالإيمان لحظة واحدة ، فإنه ينعزل عن الإلهية ، ويصير سفيها مستحقاً للذم . وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون مالكاً ، بل يكون مملوكاً [ بل يكون حاله أشد من حال مملوك ] لأن المملوك قد يتوقع الخلاص عن ذل تكليف المولى . والله تعالى لا رضاء له [ البنّة في الخلاص ] عن ذلك التكليف
فيلزم أن لا يكون تعالى مالكاً ليوم الدين ، بل يكون مملوكاً في يوم الدين ـ تعالى الله عنه علواً كبيراً -
الثاني : إن كفر العبد ومعصيته متناهية . والعقل يقضي أن يكون العقاب بقدر الجرم . لا زائداً عليه ، فوجب أن يكون عقاب الكفر والمعصية متناهياً [ فلما جعل عقاباته غير متناهية ] علمنا أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : أحكام أفعاله على أحكام أفعالنا في الحسن والقبح . وذلك يبطل قول المعتزلة .
الثالث : إن المحاسبة على المأكول والمشروب مستقبحة من العظماء وجميع الدنيا بالنسبة إلى خزائن الله أقل من الذرة بالنسبة إلى البحر الأعظم . فلو كانت أحكامه مثل أحكامنا ، لوجب أن يقبح منه حساب العبد في الذرة واللقمة . ولما لم يقبح ، علمنا : أنه لا تقاس أفعاله على أفعالنا ، ولا أحكامه على أحكامنا . وذلك يبطل الاعتزال بالكلية .
On (the verse): 'Owner of the Day of Judgment'. The Mu'tazila said: The religion (dīn) is recompense. And recompense is only good based on action. So if the servant has no action, it is impossible to attribute recompense to him. So it is necessary that He is the Owner of the Day of Judgment. Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: That He, exalted, is the Owner of the Day of Judgment is only complete according to our position. And its clarification is from several aspects: First: We assume a man who persisted in his disbelief for eighty years, then it happens that he believes, and dies immediately after his belief. This man disobeyed God by disbelief throughout his entire life, then he performed belief for one moment. Then he would be stripped of divinity and become foolish, deserving of blame. On this estimation, He would not be an owner, but rather owned—rather his state would be worse than that of an owned (slave)—because the slave may hope for deliverance from the humiliation of the master's obligation, while God has no pleasure at all in deliverance from that obligation. So it follows that He, exalted, would not be the Owner of the Day of Judgment, but rather would be owned on the Day of Judgment—exalted is God above that, greatly. Second: The disbelief and disobedience of the servant are finite. Reason judges that punishment is proportionate to the sin, not exceeding it. So it is necessary that the punishment for disbelief and disobedience is finite. Since He made His punishments infinite, we know that it cannot be said: His judgments concerning actions are like our judgments concerning good and evil. And that invalidates the statement of the Mu'tazila. Third: Accounting for food and drink is considered ugly from great people, and all of the world compared to the treasuries of God is less than an atom compared to the great sea. So if His judgments were like our judgments, it would be necessary that it be ugly for Him to account for the servant regarding an atom and a morsel. And since it is not ugly, we know that His actions are not measured by our actions, nor His judgments by our judgments. And that invalidates Mu'tazilism entirely.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The Mu'tazila first argue from the verse 'Owner of the Day of Judgment' that since reward and punishment (al-dīn) are based on action, the servant must have free will; otherwise, God could not justly be the owner of that day. Ahl al-Sunna respond with three proofs to show that God's ownership is only consistent with their position (that God creates all actions).

First proof (al-awwal): A reductio. Consider a man who disbelieves for eighty years then believes and dies immediately. On the Mu'tazila view, this man would be rewarded for his brief faith and punished for his long disbelief. But this would make God's ownership absurd: He would be 'owned' (mamlūk) instead of Owner, since the man's actions would control God's judgment. The absurdity shows that human actions cannot be the basis for God's recompense.

Second proof (al-thānī): The servant's sins are finite, so punishment should be finite. But God's punishment for disbelief is eternal (infinite). If God's judgments were like ours, infinite punishment for finite sin would be unjust. Since it is not unjust, God's judgments are incomparable. This refutes the Mu'tazila analogy.

Third proof (al-thālith): Holding humans accountable for minor things (like food and drink) is considered disgraceful for great people, but God's treasures are infinite—so such accounting from Him is not disgraceful. Therefore, God's judgments cannot be measured by human standards. This entirely invalidates Mu'tazilism.

Each proof concludes by refuting the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
مالك يوم الدين
الدين
الجزاء
الفعل
المعتزلة
أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that God's ownership of the Day of Judgment proves human free will and that God's justice requires recompense based on action.
Correction: The Sunni proof shows that such a view leads to absurd consequences, like God being 'owned' by a servant's actions, which is impossible. Therefore, God's ownership must be understood differently.

Checkpoint
In the first Sunni proof, what consequence follows if a disbeliever of 80 years believes at the last moment?
A) God would be praised for His mercy.
B) The man would be justly punished for his disbelief.
C) God would become 'owned' on the Day of Judgment.
D) The man's faith would be worthless.

Key Takeaway
The first Sunni proof refutes the Mu'tazila by showing their understanding of God's ownership leads to the absurdity that God could be 'owned' by human actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The reductio shows that if God's judgment depended on your actions, He would be enslaved by them. Do you live as though your deeds control God's response? How does recognizing His absolute independence change the way you approach repentance and hope?
?
If God were 'owned' by human actions, every sin would be catastrophic and every good deed a negotiation. Does the Sunni position free you from the anxiety of earning God's favor? Will you trust in His sovereignty rather than your own performance?
?
The man in the example disbelieves for eighty years but is saved by a moment of faith. On the Mu'tazila view, that moment forces God to forgive. On the Sunni view, God forgives out of His own will. Which view makes you more grateful? How does this affect your worship today?

Second Sunni Proof: Finite Sin vs Infinite Punishment
Second Sunni proof: finite sin vs infinite punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Sunni proof: since human sins are finite but God's punishment for disbelief is infinite, God's judgments cannot be measured by human standards.

Core Idea
Finite sin cannot justify infinite punishment unless Divine judgments transcend human analogy.

The second proof addresses the Mu'tazila assumption that God's justice resembles human justice. It points out that human sins are finite, yet God punishes disbelief with eternal punishment. If God's judgments were like ours, infinite punishment for finite sin would be unjust. Since it is not unjust, the Mu'tazila analogy collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic from 'الثاني : إن كفر العبد ومعصيته متناهية' to 'وذلك يبطل قول المعتزلة'. Notice that it starts with two premises: sins are finite, reason demands proportionate punishment.
2
Understand the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: if punishment must equal sin, then eternal punishment for finite sin would be unjust. But God gives eternal punishment, so His judgments are not bound by human notions of proportionality.
This refutes the Mu'tazila use of 'al-husn wa al-qubh' (good and evil).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes: 'وذلك يبطل قول المعتزلة' — this invalidates the Mu'tazila statement. Understand why.

According to the second proof, why cannot God's judgments be compared to human judgments?

?

?

?

Third Sunni Proof: God's Transcendence Over Human Norms
Third Sunni proof: God's transcendence over human norms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Sunni proof: God's accounting for minor things is not disgraceful because His treasures are infinite, unlike human rulers.

Core Idea
If God judged like humans, accounting for a morsel would be petty; but God is infinitely great, so it is not.

The third proof uses a simple analogy: among humans, it is considered disgraceful for great people to hold others accountable for trivial matters like food and drink. But God's treasures are infinite, so such accounting from Him is not disgraceful. This shows that God's actions cannot be measured by human standards, completely invalidating the Mu'tazila position.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic from 'الثالث : إن المحاسبة على المأكول والمشروب' to the end. Notice the comparison: human accountability for minor things is ugly, but God's infinite treasures make it not so.
2
Understand the analogy
The proof draws a parallel: a great human king would be considered petty if he counted every morsel eaten by his subjects. But God, whose treasures are infinitely greater than the entire world, is not petty for doing so.
This refutes the Mu'tazila assumption that God's actions must align with human notions of decorum.
3
See the conclusion
The proof ends: 'وذلك يبطل الاعتزال بالكلية' — this entirely invalidates Mu'tazilism. Understand why it is a complete refutation.

Second proof (al-thānī): The servant's sins are finite, so punishment should be finite. But God's punishment for disbelief is eternal (infinite). If God's judgments were like ours, infinite punishment for finite sin would be unjust. Since it is not unjust, God's judgments are incomparable. This refutes the Mu'tazila analogy.

Third proof (al-thālith): Holding humans accountable for minor things (like food and drink) is considered disgraceful for great people, but God's treasures are infinite—so such accounting from Him is not disgraceful. Therefore, God's judgments cannot be measured by human standards. This entirely invalidates Mu'tazilism.

Each proof concludes by refuting the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
المحاسبة
الحسن والقبح
الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that what is morally good or bad for humans is also good or bad for God (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyān).
Correction: The third proof shows that if that were true, God's accounting for minor things would be disgraceful, but it is not; hence God's morality is not bound by human norms.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof use to show that God's judgments are not like human judgments?
A) The fact that God is all-powerful.
B) The fact that God's treasures are infinitely greater than the entire world.
C) The fact that humans are sinful.
D) The fact that the Day of Judgment will be long.

Key Takeaway
The third proof completes the refutation by showing that God's infinite greatness makes His accounting of trivial matters not disgraceful, proving His judgments are incomparable to human judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that God is being 'unfair' about something small in your life, do you realize you are measuring His actions by human standards? How can you train yourself to submit to His judgment instead of demanding explanation?
?
Does your heart ever whisper that God is too great to care about your small struggles? How does this lesson correct that? What will you do when you feel that whisper again?
?
If you truly believe God's judgments are incomparable, what small matter are you holding against Him that you need to release right now? Will you consciously entrust it to His wisdom today?

The Mu'tazila's Argument from 'You Alone We Worship'
Mu'tazila argue free will from worship verse; Sunni counter-argument starts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's rational objection to divine creation of human actions based on the verse 'You alone we worship' and the opening of the Sunni response invoking the concept of acquisition (kasb).

Core Idea
If God creates worship, our striving is redundant; but the verse implies free will.

The Mu'tazila launch a powerful objection rooted in a daily-recited verse. If God alone creates actions, then our act of worship would be trying to obtain what already exists—a logical impossibility. This forces a choice: either the verse is false, or we have independent agency. The Sunni response begins by affirming that worship does come into existence, but the full counter-argument will unfold in subsequent lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the Mu'tazila's logical structure.
Notice the concise style and key term 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil'.
2
Identify the core argument
Identify the concept of 'achieving what is already achieved' and how it makes worship impossible if God creates.
3
Examine the implications
Understand why the Mu'tazila conclude that the verse would be false if God creates actions, and what that means for free will.
4
Note the Sunni opening
Observe how the Sunni response begins with 'Worship only comes into existence' and consider what might follow.
This foreshadows the concept of kasb (acquisition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع
في
قيل: (إياك نعبد)
قالت المعتزلة : هذا تصريح بكون العبد فاعلاً ومستقلاً بأفعال نفسه . فإنه لو كان فعل العبد قد خلقه الله ، لكان سعى العبد جارياً بجري تحصيل الحاصل . وذلك محال . وحينئذ يبقى قوله : ( إياك نعبد ) : كذباً محضاً : لو كانت العبادة خلقها الله تعالى ، لوجب أن يقول العبد : إياك نسأل منك خلق العبادة
وأيضاً
قبال أهل السنة والجماعة : إنما تصير [ العبادة ] داخله في الوجود
Chapter Seven
On (the verse): 'You alone we worship.'
The Mu'tazila said: This is a declaration that the servant is an agent and independent in his own actions. For if the action of the servant were created by God, then the servant's striving would be pursuing the attainment of what is already attained, which is impossible. And then His statement 'You alone we worship' would be pure falsehood: if worship were created by God, it would be necessary that the servant say: 'From You we ask the creation of worship.' And also
Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: Worship only comes into existence...
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter (Chapter Seven) and presents the Mu'tazila's objection based on the verse 'You alone we worship' (Iyyāka na'budu). The Mu'tazila argue that this verse clearly indicates the servant is an independent agent (fāʿil mustaqill). If God created all actions, then the servant's act of worship would be a 'seeking to attain what is already attained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. Consequently, the verse would be false, because the servant would be asking for the creation of worship rather than performing it. The phrase 'and also' (wa ayḍan) suggests an additional point, but it is not elaborated. The beginning of the Sunni response is then given: 'Worship only comes into existence...' (innamā taṣīru al-ʿibāda dākhilan fī al-wujūd), but the argument is incomplete due to the page break. The Mu'tazila argument hinges on the impossibility of 'acquiring what is already obtained'—if God creates the action, the servant's effort is redundant.

العبادة

Misconception: The Mu'tazila argument seems logically airtight, so God must not create actions.
Correction: The Sunni response, though incomplete here, argues that through kasb (acquisition) the action is both created by God and attributed to the servant without contradiction.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila interpret 'You alone we worship' as proof that humans create their own actions, because if God created them, worship would be a logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

The Mu'tazila Argument from 'Ihdina' and the Sunni Response
Mu'tazila and Sunni arguments from the verse on guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's linguistic argument from the verse 'Guide us to the straight path' and why Ahl al-Sunna reject reducing guidance to mere indication.

Core Idea
Guidance in the Qur'an means more than showing the way—it is Allah's act of creating faith.

In this passage, we encounter a classic theological debate over the meaning of a single word: 'ihdina' (guide us). The Mu'tazila argue that the verse proves human ability to seek and attain guidance, while Ahl al-Sunna insist that true guidance is a divine creation, not just information. The passage presents both sides, though the Sunni response is cut off mid-sentence.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice that it first presents the Mu'tazila view, then begins the Sunni rebuttal.
The Sunni response is incomplete on this page; we only have the beginning.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's two arguments
Find the two reasons the Mu'tazila give: (1) The word 'ihdina' is a request, showing the servant is an agent. (2) Requesting guidance is only proper if the servant can be guided, and 'huda' linguistically means 'indication and explanation', not 'creation of faith'. Note their proof from the Thamud verse.
3
Analyze the Sunni counter-argument
The Sunni response begins by stating that the verse is among the strongest proofs for their doctrine. Their first point is that guidance cannot be reduced to mere explanation because Allah already provides that universally—so the prayer must ask for something more, like the creation of guidance in the heart.
This directly refutes the Mu'tazila's linguistic definition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع في (اهدنا الصراط المستقيم. صراط الذين أنعمت عليهم) قالت المعتزلة : هذه الآية دالة على قولنا من وجهين : الأول : إن قوله : ( إهدنا ) : طلب . وهذا تصريح بكون العبد فاعلاً لهذا الطلب . الثاني : إن طلب الهداية إنما يحسن إذا كان العبد يصح منه أن يهندي بهداه. إذ الهدى هو الدلالة والبيان . ولا يجوز المراد بقوله : ( اهدنا ) أي اخلق لنا الإيمان لأن الهدى في اللغة عبارة عن الدلالة والبيان ، لا عن خلق العلم . بدليل قوله تعالى : ( وأما ثمود فهديناهم . فاستحبوا العمى على الهدى ) . قال أهل السنة والجماعة : هذه الآية من أدل الدلائل على مذهبنا من وجهين : الأول : إنه لا يمكن حمل هذه الهداية على البيان والدلالة . لأن الله تعالى
Chapter Nine on (Guide us to the straight path. The path of those upon whom You have bestowed favor). The Mu'tazila said: This verse indicates our doctrine in two ways: First: His statement (Guide us) is a request, and this is an explicit declaration that the servant is the agent of this request. Second: Requesting guidance is only proper if the servant can rightly be guided by his own guidance, for guidance (huda) is indication and explanation. It is not permissible to intend by His statement (Guide us) meaning 'create faith for us', because guidance in language is indication and explanation, not creation of knowledge. Evidence is His statement: (And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance). Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: This verse is among the strongest evidences for our doctrine in two ways: First: It is impossible to interpret this guidance as mere explanation and indication, because Allah the Exalted...
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents the argument regarding the verse 'Ihdina al-sirat al-mustaqim' (Guide us to the straight path). The Mu'tazila advance two points: (1) The word 'ihdina' is a request, showing the servant is an agent. (2) Requesting guidance is only reasonable if the servant can potentially be guided by his own capacity; they argue 'huda' linguistically means 'indication and explanation', not 'creation of faith', citing the Thamud verse where guidance was given but they chose misguidance.

Ahl al-Sunnah respond that this verse is actually among the strongest proofs for their own doctrine. They begin their response here (the text cuts off at the end of the page). Their first argument is that 'guidance' cannot be reduced to mere explanation and indication, because Allah has already provided that universally; rather, the prayer asks for something more: the creation of guidance in the heart, which only Allah can do. This implies human inability to guide themselves, contradicting the Mu'tazilite claim.

Note: The passage on this page is incomplete; the full Ahl al-Sunnah response continues on the next page. The commentary is based solely on the available text.

بيان

Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that 'guidance' in this verse only means 'showing the way', and that humans can guide themselves by their own capacity.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah argue that true guidance includes the divine act of creating faith in the heart, which humans cannot achieve on their own. The verse's request implies human need for Allah's direct intervention.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila define guidance as mere indication, but Ahl al-Sunna argue that true guidance is a divine creation beyond indication, as seen in the prayer 'Guide us'.

Refuting the Mu'tazila using the Verse 'Ghayr al-Maghdubi 'Alayhim'
Refutation of Mu'tazilite interpretation using verse order and rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the order of phrases in Quranic verses can indicate causality and refute the Mu'tazilite doctrine of autonomous human will.

Core Idea
The verse's order reveals that divine wrath causes misguidance, not human choice alone.

This passage presents a classic Sunni refutation of the Mu'tazilite interpretation of a key verse from Surat al-Fatiha. The Mu'tazila argued that the verse supports their belief in human free will; the author uses the very same verse, along with rational argument and principles of jurisprudence, to demonstrate that divine causation precedes and necessitates human actions. This lesson walks through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila Claim
Start by reading the Mu'tazilite objection: they argue that if the servant had no power, God's wrath would be unjust, and the phrase 'nor those who are astray' attributes misguidance to them, not God.
Notice how they use the verse to support free will.
2
Follow the Sunni Rational Argument
Read the Sunni response: the servant is capable of both faith and disbelief, but a preponderating factor from God is needed. When God creates a preponderance towards disbelief, that is the wrath, and misguidance follows as its effect.
This is the core rational proof.
3
Understand the Usul al-Fiqh Principle
The author invokes the principle that ordering a ruling after an appropriate description indicates causality. Here, being 'astray' follows being 'an object of wrath', so wrath is the cause.
This connects the verse's wording to logical causality.
4
Analyze the Analogies
Examine the analogies from other verses: God's pleasure causes human pleasure, and His love causes human love. They reinforce that divine attributes are effective, not vice versa.
This refutes any reversal of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الساهر في قوله تعالى: (غير المغضوب عليهم ولا الضالين ) قالت المعتزلة : هذا إنما يتم على قولنا . لأن العبد لو لم يقدر على الفعل لكان غضب الله عليه محض الظلم . وأيضاً قوله : ( ولا الضالين ) يقتضي إسناد الضلال إليهم ، لا إلى الله تعالى . قال أهل السنة والجماعة : هذه الآية دالة على قولنا . لإنه تعالى وصفهم بكونهم مغضوباً عليهم ، ثم ذكر عقيبه كونهم ضالين . وهذا يدل على أن كونهم مغضوباً عليهم ، هو المؤثر في كونهم ضالين . والذي يدل عليه : أن العبد لما كان قادراً على الإيمان والكفر ، امتنع أن يقدم على الإيمان بدلاً عن الكفر ، وبالعكس ، إلا المرجح من قبل الله تعالى. فإذا خلق الله فيه مرجع الكفر فذلك هو غضب الله عليه . والضلال بعد ذلك هو أثر ذلك الغضب ونتيجته وثمرته . فثبت بهذا البرهان العقلي . أن كونه مغضوباً عليه هو الموجب لصيرورته من الضالين . واللفظ أيضاً مشعور به . لأنه ثبت في أصول الفقه أن ترتيب الحكم على الوصف المناسب مشعر بالعلية . وكونه ضالاً يناسب أن يكون معللاً بكونه مغضوباً عليه . وهو مذكور عقيبه . فيوجب كونه معللا به ، وذلك يبطل القول بالاعتزال. ونظير هذا قوله تعالى : ( رضي الله عنهم ، ورضوا عنه ) فإن كونه تعالى راضيا عنهم ، هو الذي أوجب صيرورتهم راضين عنه . وهذا هو الواجب ، لتكون صفة الحق مؤثرة في صفة الخلق . أما لو كان كوننا راضين عنه يؤثر في كونه تعالى راضياً عنا ، لكانت صفة الخلق مؤثرة في صفة الحق . وذلك محال . ونظيره أيضاً قوله تعالى : ( يحبهم ويحبونه ) والله أعلم
The chapter on His saying: (Not those who have incurred wrath, nor those who are astray). The Mu'tazilites said: This only holds according to our view, because if the servant had no power over the action, then God's wrath upon him would be pure injustice. Also His saying: (nor those who are astray) necessitates attributing misguidance to them, not to God. The People of the Sunnah and the Community said: This verse indicates our view, because He described them as being objects of wrath, then mentioned after it their being astray. And this indicates that their being objects of wrath is the effective cause in their being astray. And what indicates it is: that when the servant is capable of faith and disbelief, it is impossible for him to proceed to faith instead of disbelief, and vice versa, except by a preponderating factor from God. So if God creates in him a preponderance of disbelief, then that is God's wrath upon him. And misguidance after that is the effect of that wrath, its result and fruit. So it is established by this rational proof that his being an object of wrath is what necessitates his becoming one of the astray. And the wording also indicates it, because it is established in the principles of jurisprudence that the ordering of a ruling upon an appropriate attribute is indicative of causality. And his being astray is appropriate to be explained by his being an object of wrath. And it is mentioned after it. So it necessitates that it is explained by it, and that invalidates the Mu'tazilite doctrine. An analogy to this is His saying: (Allah is pleased with them and they are pleased with Him). For His being pleased with them is what necessitated their being pleased with Him. And this is what is necessary, so that the attribute of the Truth is effective in the attribute of the creation. But if our being pleased with Him affected His being pleased with us, then the attribute of the creation would be effective in the attribute of the Truth, which is impossible. Another analogy is His saying: (He loves them and they love Him). And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite interpretation of the Quranic verse 'ghayr al-maghdubi 'alayhim wa la al-dallin' (not those who have incurred wrath, nor those who are astray). It begins by stating the Mu'tazilite objection: they argue that the verse supports their doctrine of human free will because if the servant had no power (qudra) over his actions, God's wrath would be unjust. Additionally, the phrase 'nor those who are astray' attributes misguidance to them, not to God. The author then presents the Sunni response, using the order of the words in the verse. He points out that God first describes them as 'those who have incurred wrath' (maghdub 'alayhim) and then immediately after mentions 'those who are astray' (dallin). This sequential order indicates causality (al-'illiyya): being an object of wrath is the effective cause in their being astray. He supports this with a rational argument: the servant is capable of both faith and disbelief, but without a preponderating factor (murajjih) from God, he will not choose one over the other. When God creates in him a preponderance towards disbelief, that is the wrath, and misguidance follows as its effect. Thus, the verse, combined with rational proof, shows that wrath precedes and causes misguidance. The author also invokes a principle from usul al-fiqh: linking a judgment (hukm) to an appropriate description (wasf munasib) indicates that the description is the cause ('illa). Here, being 'astray' follows the description 'object of wrath' appropriately, so wrath is the cause. He then provides two analogies from other verses: 'Allah is pleased with them and they are pleased with Him' – God's pleasure causes their pleasure, not vice versa; and 'He loves them and they love Him' – His love causes their love. If the reverse were true, it would imply that created attributes affect the divine, which is impossible. This refutes the Mu'tazilite position of autonomous human causality.

Key Terms
المغضوب عليهم
الضالين
المعتزلة
أهل السنة والجماعة
العلية
ترتيب الحكم على الوصف المناسب
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse attributes misguidance solely to human choice without any divine causation.
Correction: The order of phrases in the verse ('those who have incurred wrath' then 'those who are astray') indicates that divine wrath is the cause of misguidance, as per the principle of causality in usul al-fiqh.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni response in this passage, what does the order of 'maghdub 'alayhim' and 'dallin' in the verse indicate?
A) That misguidance causes God's wrath.
B) That wrath and misguidance are unrelated.
C) That being an object of wrath is the cause of being astray.
D) That the verse does not prove causality.

Key Takeaway
The order of descriptors in Quranic verses can indicate causal relationships, and this passage demonstrates how divine wrath is the cause of human misguidance, refuting the Mu'tazilite view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself slipping into sin, do you recognize that it is only by God's preponderating factor that you could turn to faith? Have you ever genuinely begged Allah to create in you a preponderance of belief before it's too late?
?
Do you truly believe that God's wrath upon someone is the cause of their misguidance? How does this change the way you view those who are astray—do you see them as merely making bad choices, or as objects of divine wrath that should evoke both fear and heartfelt compassion?
?
If God's pleasure with us must precede our pleasure with Him, then what does that mean for your relationship with Allah right now? Are you waiting for signs before you submit, or are you actively seeking His pleasure first, knowing that your delight in Him depends on His prior delight in you?

The Role of Reason in Deciding the Free Will Debate
Author concludes the debate, asserting the necessity of rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational proofs are necessary for decisive preponderance in theological debates, as shown in the author's conclusion.

Core Idea
Quranic evidence is broad, but rational proofs tip the scale.

After presenting the debate between Jabriyya and Qadariyya, the author concludes by highlighting the limitation of purely scriptural arguments. While the whole Quran can be invoked by both sides, only intellectual proofs can provide decisive resolution. This lesson unpacks that conclusion and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice how the author summarizes the debate and then states his conclusion.
The passage is short but dense with meaning.
2
Identify the key claims
Pick out the three main points: 1) The Quran can be used by both sides, 2) Decisive preponderance requires rational proofs, 3) The author's side has stronger rational proofs.
3
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why the author emphasizes rational proofs over textual evidence alone. What does this teach us about resolving theological disputes?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا إشارة مختصرة إلى المناظرة الجارية بين الجبرية وبين القدرية . ومن وقف على ذلك أمكنه استنباط الدلائل من الجانبين ، وعند هذا يصير كل القرآن صالحاً لأن يتمسك به هذا . ولا يكاد يظهر الترجيح القاطع إلا بالرجوع إلى الدلائل العقلية وقد عرفت قوة الدلائل العقلية من جانبنا . فثبت : أن قولنا هو الحق ، ومذهبنا هو الصدق والله أعلم
This is a brief reference to the debate that took place between the Jabriyya and the Qadariyya. Whoever examines this can deduce proofs from both sides. And at this point, the entire Quran becomes suitable to be used as evidence. But the decisive preponderance cannot be shown except by returning to intellectual proofs, and you have known the strength of the intellectual proofs on our side. So it is established that our statement is the truth, and our school is the truth. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is the author's conclusion of a previous debate between the Jabriyya (those who affirm absolute predestination) and the Qadariyya (those who affirm human free will). The author states that their summary allows one to derive arguments from both sides, and that the whole Quran can be used as evidence. However, he asserts that decisive preference cannot be attained except through intellectual proofs, and that the strength of rational arguments supports his own position. He concludes that his view is the truth and that his school is correct. The passage emphasizes the role of reason in settling theological disputes.

Key Terms
الجبرية
القدرية
الترجيح القاطع
الدلائل العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Quranic evidence alone suffices to settle the debate without rational reasoning.
Correction: The author explicitly states that decisive preponderance cannot appear except by returning to intellectual proofs, implying that scriptural evidence alone is not decisive.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is required for decisive preponderance in the debate between Jabriyya and Qadariyya?
A) Only Quranic verses
B) Only Hadith
C) Intellectual proofs
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The decisive factor in theological debates about free will is not the abundance of scriptural proofs but the strength of rational arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about free will and divine decree, can you defend it with rational arguments, or do you only rely on memorized verses? Will you commit to studying rational proofs so that your faith is not just inherited but intellectually grounded?
?
The author declares his school is the truth because of stronger rational proofs. If you were challenged to prove your position, would your arguments hold up? What are you doing to ensure your convictions are built on reason, not just tradition?
?
Is there a theological issue where you have avoided intellectual scrutiny because it feels uncomfortable? Ask yourself: Am I willing to face rational arguments honestly, even if they challenge my current views, for the sake of truth?

Mu'tazilite Quranic Arguments: Attributing Actions to Servants
Mu'tazilites cite Quranic verses that attribute actions to humans to prove free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first type of Mu'tazilite Quranic arguments where verses attribute actions directly to humans, and how these verses challenge the deterministic view.

Core Idea
Quranic attribution of actions to humans is literal evidence of free will.

The Mu'tazilites extensively used Quranic verses to support their doctrine of free will. This lesson introduces the first aspect: verses that explicitly attribute actions like faith, prayer, and disbelief to human beings. We will examine three examples and see how the author contrasts them with the determinist (Jabri) interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage opening
The author introduces the third type of Mu'tazilite arguments and the first aspect: words attributing actions to servants. Read how he sets the stage.
2
Examine the first example (verse A)
Note the verse from Surah Al-Baqarah about believers. The author points out that Allah attributes faith, prayer, and spending directly to them, not saying 'created in them'.
3
Examine the second example (verse B)
The verse about disbelievers: faith is dependent on their will. See how this implies they choose not to believe.
4
Examine the third example (verse C)
The hypocrites' deception is attributed to themselves, not to Allah. The author notes the Jabri alternative would be absurd.
5
Reflect on the pattern
All three examples show the Quran ascribing actions to human agents, which the Mu'tazilites take as proof of free will.

This passage introduces the third type of Mu'tazilite arguments from the Quran. The author notes that these arguments are many, but he will summarize their main points. Proof of Human Agency and Refutation of Kasb
Proof of human agency through exhaustive dichotomy and refutation of acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dichotomy used to prove that the servant creates his own actions, and why the concept of acquisition (kasb) is rejected as a false middle ground.

Core Idea
Either the servant creates his actions or the Quranic verses calling him an agent are false.

In this passage, the author presents a decisive argument for human agency. He argues that our actions must be created either by God or by ourselves. If God alone creates them, then the Quranic verses that attribute actions to humans would be meaningless. Since that is impossible, we must be the ones who bring our actions into existence. He then addresses and dismisses the notion of 'acquisition' as an empty term.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dichotomy
The author sets up a clear either/or: actions come from non-existence into existence either by God's power or the servant's power. Recognize this as an exhaustive division.
2
Examine the first option
If actions are solely by God's power, then the servant has no power over them, making the Quranic verses that call humans agents false. The author considers this absurd.
3
Accept the second option
Since the first option leads to falsehood, the only logical conclusion is that the servant himself originates his actions.
4
Refute the kasb objection
The author rejects the idea of acquisition as an intermediate category, repeating the dichotomy and showing that any middle ground is not genuinely intermediate.
The term 'kasb' is obscure and introduces confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطريق الاستدلال بالكل : أن نقول : خروج هذه الأفعال من العدم إلى الوجود . إما أن يكون بقدرة الله تعالى ، أو بقدرة العبد . فإن كان بقدرة الله لم يكن للعبد قدرة ، لا على وجودها ، ولا على عدمها . لإن الله تعالى إذا خلقها فهي حاصلة . سواء أراد العبد ذلك ، أو لم يرده . وإذا لم يخلقها ، لم يقدر العبد على تحصيلها . فثبت أن حصول الفعل ، لو كان بقدرة الله لم يبق للعبد إليه اختيار ومكنة ، وعند هذا لا يكون الفعل فعلاً للعبد ، وحينئذ تصير هذه الآيات الدالة على كونه فاعلاً ، كلها أكاذيب وأباطيل وأضاليل . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً . علمنا أن العبد موجد الأفعال نفسه . ولا يقال : العبد وإن لم يكن موجداً الأفعال نفسه، لكنه مكتسب لها . لأنا نقول : هذا اللفظ غير ملخص المعنى
·
إلا أنا ذكرنا في الدليل : أن المؤثر في إخراج (۲) هذه الأفعال من العدم إلى الوجود . إما أن يكون هو العبد ، أو لا يكون . فإن كان هو العبد فقد حصل المطلوب . وإن لم يكن هو العبد ، لم يكن العبد على هذا التقدير متمكناً لا من الفعل ، ولا من الترك. وذلك يقتضي كون هذه الآيات أكاذيب . وإذا كان لا واسطة بين النفي وبين الإثبات - ونحن أبطلنا أحد القسمين - فحينئذ يبقى [ الثاني (٣) ] لا محالة . فكان إلقاء لفظ ( الكسب، في هذا الموضوع : محض التزوير .
And the way of inference from the whole [of such verses] is that we say: The emergence of these actions from non-existence into existence is either by the power of God Most High, or by the power of the servant. If it is by the power of God, then the servant has no power, neither over their existence nor over their non-existence. Because if God Most High creates them, then they occur, whether the servant wills that or does not will it. And if He does not create them, the servant cannot bring them about. So it is established that if the occurrence of the action were by the power of God, the servant would have no choice and no ability regarding it, and then the action would not be an action of the servant, and at that point these verses that indicate that he is an agent would all become lies, falsehoods, and delusions. And since that is false, we know that the servant is the originator of the actions himself. And it is not said: The servant, even if he is not the originator of the actions himself, yet he acquires them. For we say: This term is not clarified in meaning, except that we mentioned in the proof: That the effective agent in bringing these actions from non-existence into existence is either the servant or not. If it is the servant, then the desired [conclusion] is obtained. And if it is not the servant, then on this assumption the servant has no ability, neither for the action nor for its abandonment, and that necessitates that these verses are lies. And since there is no intermediate between negation and affirmation—and we have invalidated one of the two divisions—then the second remains necessarily. So the introduction of the term 'acquisition' (al-kasb) in this context is pure falsification.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a proof for human agency in actions, followed by a refutation of the Ash'arite concept of acquisition (kasb). The author begins by stating the method of inference from Quranic verses that attribute actions to humans. He establishes a dichotomy: every action's coming into existence is either by God's power or by the servant's power. If by God's power, then the servant has no power over the action's existence or non-existence—God creates it regardless of the servant's will, and if God doesn't create it, the servant cannot produce it. Consequently, if actions were solely God's act, the servant would have no choice or ability, making the Quranic verses that call humans 'agents' false. Since that is impossible, the only alternative is that the servant himself originates his actions. The author then addresses a potential objection: that the servant, while not originating, 'acquires' the action. He dismisses the term 'acquisition' as obscure and repeats his proof in another form: the effective cause of the action is either the servant or not. If the servant is the cause, the point is proven; if not, the servant has no ability for action or abandonment, rendering the verses false. Since there is no middle ground between negation and affirmation, and one side (divine causation alone) has been refuted, the servant must be the efficient cause. Therefore, introducing the term 'acquisition' is mere falsification.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
كسب (acquisition)
فاعل (agent)
إخراج من العدم إلى الوجود (bringing from non-existence to existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The servant can 'acquire' actions without creating them, as a middle ground between divine creation and human creation.
Correction: The author argues that such a middle ground does not exist: the effective cause of an action's existence either is the servant or not. If it is not the servant, the servant has no ability, contradicting the Quranic attribution of agency.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the author draw from the dichotomy that actions are either by God or by the servant?
A) The servant has no power regardless.
B) The servant creates his own actions.
C) Actions are caused by both God and the servant equally.
D) The Quranic verses are metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The author proves human agency by an exhaustive dichotomy: if God alone created actions, the Quranic attribution of agency would be false; since that is impossible, humans must be the creators of their own actions, and the term 'acquisition' is an invalid intermediate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you truly believe that you are the originator (mujid) of your actions, how does that change the way you approach your daily choices, especially the small ones you usually ignore? Will you consciously own one action today as entirely yours, and what will it be?

The author argues that if God alone created actions, the Quranic verses calling you an agent would be false. Do you live as if your actions are ultimately your own, or do you sometimes excuse yourself by thinking 'God willed it' to avoid responsibility? What would it look like to fully accept that you are the doer, even in your failures?
?
Consider an area in your life where you feel powerless to change—a habit or situation you attribute to fate. Based on this proof, is that feeling of powerlessness a contradiction to your theology? How can you reclaim your agency in that specific area, starting today, even if just in intention?

Reconciling Quranic Attributions with Divine Decree
The author offers a reconciliation between Quranic attributions and divine decree based on his school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Māturīdi school reconciles Quranic verses that attribute actions to humans with the doctrine that everything is by divine decree.

Core Idea
The combination of power with motive, created by God, entails the action, harmonizing human attribution with divine decree.

The Quran is filled with verses attributing actions to humans, yet rational proofs show all is by God's decree. The author presents a reconciliation based on his school: the combination of power and motive is the effective cause of action, and this combination is created by God. This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Recognize that the Quran contains many verses attributing actions to humans, but rational proofs indicate everything is by divine decree. This creates an apparent contradiction that needs resolution.
2
Learn the proposed solution
The author introduces 'the combination of power with motive' as the effective cause of action. This combination is created by God and entails the action, making both human attribution and divine decree valid.
Key Arabic term: مجموع القدرة مع الداعي
3
Note the school-specific condition
The reconciliation only works if one holds that the combination of power and motive is necessitating (mustalzim) for the action. Those who reject this will find it difficult to affirm these verses.
This reflects the Māturīdi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : لا شك أن القرآن مملوء من هذا النوع من الآيات . فإنكارها يكون إنكاراً للقرآن ، وقد دلت الدلائل القاهرة على أن الكل بقضاء الله وقدره ، فلا بد من التوفيق بين البابين . فنقول : مجموع القدرة مع الداعي مؤثر في الفعل ، وخالق ذلك المجموع هو الله تعالى ولكون هذا المجموع مستلزما لحصول الفعل ، صحت هذه الإضافات والإسنادات . ولكون ذلك المجموع ، موجباً لوقوع هذه الأفعال، صح أن الكل بقضاء الله وبقدره ، وبهذا الطريق يزول التناقض بين الدلائل العقلية وبين هذه الدلائل القرآنية قال مولانا الداعي إلى الله : واعلم : إن هذا إنما يتم على مذهبي خاصة . حيث قلت : مجموع القدرة مع الداعي مستلزم للفعل ، أما من لا يقول بذلك فإنه يتعذر عليه التصديق بهذه الآيات .
And the answer: There is no doubt that the Quran is filled with this type of verse. Denying them would be a denial of the Quran, and compelling proofs have indicated that everything is by God's decree and predestination (qadā' wa qadar). So there must be reconciliation between the two aspects. So we say: The combination of power with motive (al-dā'ī) is the effective cause in the action, and the Creator of that combination is God Most High. Because this combination entails the occurrence of the action, these attributions and ascriptions are valid. And because that combination causes the occurrence of these actions, it is correct that everything is by God's decree and predestination. And by this way the contradiction between the rational proofs and these Quranic evidences is removed. Our master, the caller to God, said: And know: This is only valid on my school specifically, where I said: The combination of power with motive entails the action. As for one who does not say that, he finds it impossible to believe in these verses.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents a reconciliation between Quranic verses that attribute actions to humans and the doctrine that everything occurs by divine decree. The author first affirms that the Quran is full of such verses, which cannot be denied, and that rational proofs show that all is by God's decree. Therefore, a reconciliation is necessary. The solution is that the 'combination of power with motive' (majmū' al-qudra ma'a al-dā'ī) is the effective cause (mu'aththir) of the action, and God is the Creator of this combination. Because this combination necessarily leads to the action, the attributions of actions to humans are valid. And because this combination is the cause, it is correct to say everything is by God's decree. This removes the apparent contradiction. The author then adds a note from his teacher (or himself) stressing that this solution only works on his school, which holds that the combination of power and motive is necessitating (mustalzim) for the action. For those who disagree, accepting these verses becomes difficult.

Key Takeaway

The Author's Reconciliation of Divine Decree and Human Action
The author presents his method of reconciling divine decree with human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's principle that the combination of power and motive, created by Allah, necessitates human action, thus resolving the apparent contradiction between rational and revealed proofs.

Core Idea
Power plus motive equals action, both attributed to God and servant.

The author introduces his unique solution to a long-standing theological problem: how can human actions be truly our own if everything is by God's decree? He argues that the key is recognizing that God creates the entire set of conditions (power and motive), and from this totality, action necessarily follows. This preserves divine causality while affirming human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the central claim: the totality of power and motive necessitates the action.
Pay attention to the term 'مجموع' (totality).
2
Identify key terms and relationships
Identify the terms 'القدرة' (power), 'الداعي' (motive), 'الفعل' (action), and note how they relate: God creates the totality, which necessitates the action.
Understand why this totality makes both attributions correct.
3
Reflect on the author's qualification
Notice the author states this solution is unique to his school. Consider why those who reject the necessitation of action by power+motive cannot accept the Quranic verses.
This highlights the importance of the principle in the author's system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

An influence on the action, and the Creator of that totality is Allah Almighty, and because this totality necessitates the occurrence of the action, these attributions and ascriptions are correct. And because that totality is a cause for the occurrence of these actions, it is correct that everything is by Allah's decree and by His measure, and by this way the contradiction between the rational proofs and these Quranic proofs is removed. Commentary
This passage presents the author's own method for reconciling the attribution of human actions to both God and the servant, thereby resolving the apparent contradiction between rational arguments and Quranic texts. The author begins by stating that God is the creator of the entire set of conditions (the 'totality') that includes the power and motive, and this totality necessitates the occurrence of the act. Because the act follows necessarily from this God-created totality, it is valid to ascribe the act to God (as its ultimate origin) and to the servant (as the one through whose power and choice it occurs). The author then quotes his own school of thought: the combination of power and motive is what necessitates the action. He notes that this solution is only valid for those who accept this principle; those who reject it cannot genuinely affirm the Quranic verses that attribute actions to the servant.

?

When you sin, do you instinctively blame God's decree, or do you own your action? Take a moment: repent for any thought that diminishes His justice or your accountability.
?

The author argues both are true, but which response springs from your heart? What must change in your understanding of qadar so that you stand with both humility and hope?

The Mu'tazila's Second Method: The Word 'العمل'
Mu'tazila enumerate eight words and give twenty verses for 'العمل'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use twenty Quranic verses containing the word 'العمل' to argue that actions are attributed to the servant, forming part of their broader linguistic proof.

Core Idea
Twenty Quranic verses with 'عمل' show human agency.

The Mu'tazila built their case for human agency on a linguistic argument: every Arabic word for action appears in the Quran attributed to the servant. They listed eight such words, beginning with 'العمل' (work). In this passage, the author presents twenty verses where 'العمل' is clearly ascribed to human beings, showing the Quran's consistent pattern. This evidence is central to their refutation of the view that only God creates actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's claim
Start by reading the opening sentence where they assert that every Arabic action-word is used in the Quran to attribute the act to the servant. Note the eight-word framework.
The eight words are listed but only the first is detailed here.
2
Skim the twenty verses
Read through the list of twenty Quranic verses. Observe the pattern: each verse uses 'عمل' in a context where the action is attributed to a human subject.
Don't memorize the verses; focus on the consistency of attribution.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Ask yourself: Why would the Mu'tazila think this proves human agency? How might an opponent respond? The commentary will clarify the logic.
This connects to the broader qadar debate.

Commentary
This passage presents the second method of the Mu'tazila for proving that actions are attributed to the servant. They argue that every word used in Arabic to denote action is also used in the Quran to attribute that action directly to the servant. The author then enumerates eight such words. The list includes verses like 'Whoever does a righteous deed, it is for himself' and many others. After listing them, he notes that these are twenty verses just for this one word, and a thorough search would reveal many more in the Quran. The purpose is to counter any claim that actions are only attributed to God.

The Second Word: 'الفعل' in the Quran
The author lists Quranic verses that use 'فعل' for human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran uses the word 'الفعل' to attribute actions to the servant, and you will see four examples from the Quran that support this.

Core Idea
The Quran uses 'الفعل' for human actions, not only for God's.

In this passage, the author continues his refutation of the Mu'tazila by examining the second word they use: 'الفعل' (action). He presents Quranic verses that directly attribute actions to the human doer. As you read, notice how each verse clearly addresses the servant as the one who performs the action. This is the first step in showing that the Mu'tazila's linguistic evidence actually supports the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author begins with 'اللفظ الثاني [ الفعل ]' to introduce the second word.
The passage is cut off at the fourth verse.
2
Identify the Quranic verses
List each verse mentioned: a) 'وما تفعلوا من خير...', b) 'ووفيت كل نفس ما عملت...', c) 'وافعلوا الخير', d) 'والذين هم للزكاة...'. Recognize that the fourth is incomplete.
3
Analyze the attribution
For each verse, note who is being addressed as the doer. In (a) 'تفعلوا' (you do), (b) 'ما عملت' (what it did), (c) 'افعلوا' (do), (d) presumably 'الذين هم للزكاة' (those who are for zakah). All attribute the act to the servant.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on why this matters: the Mu'tazila claim that 'الفعل' in the Quran shows humans create their own actions. But the Ash'aris argue that these verses only show acquisition (kasb), not creation. This passage starts the evidence for the second word.

In this lesson, we examine the third key linguistic term used in the Qur'an to describe human action: aṣ-ṣun‘ (making). By looking at two verses that use this term, the author further supports the idea that humans are genuine agents of their deeds, a central point in the debate on qadar.

The Fourth Term: Al-Kasb (Earning)
Author cites five Quranic verses on al-kasb to affirm human earning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic term al-kasb and how the cited verses affirm that humans earn their actions and are recompensed accordingly.

Core Idea
Al-kasb: actions are earned by humans and recompensed by Allah.

In this lesson, we explore the fourth linguistic term in the discussion of human actions: al-kasb (earning). The author cites five Quranic verses that use the root k-s-b to demonstrate that humans genuinely earn their deeds. These verses tie earning directly to reward and punishment, reinforcing individual responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the term
Read the Arabic passage and note that the author introduces 'al-kasb' as the fourth term. Then look at each verse cited.
2
Examine each verse and notice the common root
For each verse, find the word derived from k-s-b. Observe the pattern: 'kasabat', 'kasab', 'yaksibu'.
All verses emphasize that earning leads to recompense.
3
Reflect on human agency implied by earning
Consider what it means for a human to 'earn' an action. How does this relate to moral accountability?
The verses show that earning results in reward or punishment.
4
Synthesize: theological significance
Summarize: al-kasb affirms that humans are genuine agents of their actions, responsible for what they earn, while Allah is the ultimate creator of actions.
This balances human responsibility and divine sovereignty.

This reinforces the idea that actions are genuinely attributable to humans.

?

Are you willing to confess this to Allah and change your earning?
?
If you were held in 'pledge' (رهين) for every deed you have earned so far, what would that pledge be? What is one urgent step you can take today to shift your earnings from debt to treasure?

The Fifth Linguistic Term: al-Ja‘l (Making/Appointing)
The author enumerates three verses to illustrate human use of al-ja‘l.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'al-ja‘l' in Arabic is used for both divine and human acts, as demonstrated by three Quranic verses where humans are the subjects of 'making'.

Core Idea
Al-ja‘l is not exclusive to Allah — humans also 'make' and 'appoint'.

We have seen several linguistic terms that support human agency. Now we look at the fifth term, al-ja‘l (making/appointing). The author cites three Quranic verses where the verb 'ja‘ala' is attributed to human beings. This shows that the concept of 'making' or 'appointing' is not restricted to divine action but is part of human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

The verses are referenced with letters A, B, C.
2
Identify the three human actions
For each verse, identify what action of 'making' or 'appointing' is attributed to humans. One is physical, two are ideological.
Verse A: physical action of placing fingers; Verse B: declaring angels female; Verse C: attributing partners to Allah.
3
Reflect on the implication for human agency
Consider why the author includes these verses. They show that humans are capable of 'making' in various ways, supporting the doctrine of human free will and responsibility.
This term is used elsewhere for divine acts, but here it's applied to human acts.

The Sixth Term: al-Khalq and Its Quranic Inference
Quranic evidence for the term al-khalq and its inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's use of the superlative 'أحسن الخالقين' implies that creation (khalq) can be attributed to beings other than Allah in a limited sense.

Core Idea
Superlatives in the Quran imply the existence of others in that category.

This lesson introduces the sixth linguistic term in our series: al-khalq (creation). The author presents a Quranic verse that uses a superlative form arguing that it logically requires the existence of other creators. By examining this inference, we learn how careful linguistic reasoning helps us understand theological concepts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two Quranic references and the analogy provided.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to the structure of the inference.
2
Understand the superlative argument
The verse 'أحسن الخالقين' (the best of creators) uses a superlative. In Arabic, a superlative like 'أحسن' (best) implies that there are others who also create, otherwise the comparison would be meaningless. This is a key logical inference.
Think of the analogy: 'Zayd is the best of the virtuous' implies other virtuous people exist.
3
Examine the second verse
The verse addressing Jesus ('إذ تخلق من الطين') uses the verb 'تخلق' (you create) showing that the action of creation can be attributed to a human prophet in a specific context.
This reinforces that the term 'khalq' is used for fashioning or forming, not ex nihilo creation.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Some might think this verse attributes divine creation to humans. However, the author clarifies that 'khalq' here means shaping or forming, not creating from nothing. This is a crucial distinction.
The Quran uses 'khalq' in multiple senses; context determines the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ السادس : الخلق . قال الله تعالى : أ - ( فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين (۱) ) وهذا يقتضي كون غيره خالقاً . كقوله : زيد أفضل الفضلاء فإنه يقتضي كون غيره فاضلاب - قال مخاطباً لعيسى : ( وإذ تخلق من الطين
The sixth term: al-khalq (creation). Allah Almighty said: A - 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (1). And this necessitates that others besides Him are creators. Like his saying: 'Zayd is the best of the virtuous' necessitates that others are virtuous. B - He said addressing Jesus: 'And when you create from clay'
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage introduces الخلق (creation) as the sixth term. The author cites the verse 'أحسن الخالقين' (best of creators) and makes a logical inference: the superlative form 'أحسن' implies that there are other creators, otherwise the comparison would be meaningless. He gives an analogy: saying 'Zayd is the best of the virtuous' (زيد أفضل الفضلاء) implies there are other virtuous people. Thus, the Quran itself indicates that humans can be described as 'creators' in a certain sense, as in the verse addressing Jesus who creates a bird from clay (إذ تخلق من الطين).

Key Terms
الخلق
أحسن الخالقين
تخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'أحسن الخالقين' means that only Allah is Creator.
Correction: The superlative form actually implies there are other creators; otherwise the comparison would be meaningless. However, the type of creation is different for humans (fashioning) versus Allah (ex nihilo).

Checkpoint
What inference does the author draw from the verse 'أحسن الخالقين'?
A) Allah alone is Creator
B) Others besides Allah are also called creators
C) The verse is metaphorical
D) Humans cannot create anything

Key Takeaway
The Quranic use of a superlative like 'أحسن الخالقين' logically implies that the term 'khalq' can be applied to others besides Allah, though in a different sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you create something—whether it's a piece of art, a meal, or even a new habit—do you see that act as a reflection of Allah's attribute of creation, or do you feel a sense of pride that distances you from Him? How can you transform that pride into gratitude for His original creation?
?
The Quran's argument here relies on a subtle linguistic point. Have you ever rushed through verses like 'أحسن الخالقين' without pausing to appreciate the depth of their logical implication? What is one step you can take this week to study the Quran's language more carefully?
?
If the term 'khalq' applies to you in some sense, then every act of shaping your life—your relationships, your work, your character—is a kind of creation. But Allah is the best of creators. In what area of your life have you settled for mediocrity when you could be seeking His standard of excellence?
